edit by wilfrid airy ba minstce 1896 preface the life of airy be essentially that of a hard-working business man and differ from that of other hard-working people only in the quality and variety of he work it be not a exciting life but it be full of interest and he work bring he into close relation with many scientific man and with many man high in the state he real business life commence after he become astronomer royal and from that time forward during the 46 year that he remain in office he be so entirely wrap up in the duty of he post that the history of the observatory be the history of he life for write he business life there be abundant material for he preserve all he correspondence and the chief source of information be as follow : -lrb- 1 -rrb- he autobiography -lrb- 2 -rrb- he annual report to the board of visitor -lrb- 3 -rrb- he print papers entitle papers by gb airy -lrb- 4 -rrb- he miscellaneous private correspondence -lrb- 5 -rrb- he letter to he wife -lrb- 6 -rrb- he business correspondence -lrb- 1 -rrb- he autobiography after the time that he become astronomer royal be as might be expect mainly a record of the scientific work carry on at the greenwich observatory : but by no means exclusively so about the time when he take charge of the observatory there be a immense development of astronomical enterprise : observatory be spring up in all direction and the astronomer royal be expect to advise upon all of the british and colonial observatory it be necessary also for he to keep in touch with the continental observatory and they work and this he do very diligently and successfully both by correspondence and personal intercourse with the foreign astronomer there be also much work on important subject more or less connect with he official duty -- such as geodetical survey work the establishment of time-ball at different place longitude determination observation of eclipse and the determination of the density of the earth lastly there be a great deal of time and work give to question not very immediately connect with he office but on which the government ask he assistance in the capacity of general scientific adviser : such be the correction of the compass in iron ship the railway gauge commission the commission for the restoration of the standard of length and weight the maine boundary lighthouse the westminster clock the london university and many other question besides those above-mentioned there be a great many subject which he take up out of sheer interest in the investigation for it may fairly be say that every subject of a distinctly practical nature which could be advance by mathematical knowledge have a interest for he : and he incessant industry enable he to find time for many of they amongst such subject be tide and tidal observation clockwork and the strain in beam and bridge a certain portion of he time be also give to lecture generally on current astronomical question for he hold it as he duty to popularize the science as far as lay in he power and he attend the meeting of the royal astronomical society with great regularity and take a very active part in the discussion and business of the society he also do much work for the royal society and -lrb- up to a certain date -rrb- for the british association all of the foregoing matter be record pretty fully in he autobiography up to the year 1861 after that date the autobiography be give in a much more abbreviated form and might rather be regard as a collection of note for he biography he private history be give very fully for the first part of he life but be very lightly touch upon during he residence at greenwich a great part of the autobiography be in a somewhat disjointed state and appear to have be form by extract from a number of different source such as official journal official correspondence and report in editing the autobiography it have be think advisable to omit a large number of short note relate to the routine work of the observatory to technical and scientific correspondence to papers communicate to various society and official business connect with they and to miscellaneous matter of minor importance these in the aggregate occupy a great deal of time and attention but from they detach nature they would have but little general interest at various place will be find short memoirs and other matter by the editor -lrb- 2 -rrb- all of he annual report to the board of visitor be attach to he autobiography and be evidently intend to be read with it and to form part of it these report be so carefully compile and be so copious that they form a very complete history of the greenwich observatory and of the work carry on there during the time that he be astronomer royal the first report contain only four page but with the constantly increase amount and range of work the report constantly increase in volume till the later report contain 21 page extract from these report relate to matter of novelty and importance and illustrate the principle which guide he in he conduct of the observatory have be incorporate with the autobiography -lrb- 3 -rrb- the print papers by gb airy be bind in 14 large quarto volume there be 518 of these papers on a great variety of subject : a list of they be append to this history as also be a list of the book that he write and one or two of the papers which be separately print they form a very important part of he life 's work and be frequently refer to in the present history they be almost all to be find in the transaction of society or in newspaper and extend over a period of 63 year -lrb- 1822 to 1885 -rrb- the progress make in certain branch of science during this long period can very fairly be trace by these papers -lrb- 4 -rrb- he private correspondence be large and like he other papers it be carefully arrange no business letter of any kind be include under this head in this correspondence letter be occasionally find either deal with matter of importance or in some way characteristic and these have be insert in this biography as already state the autobiography leave by airy be confine almost entirely to science and business and touch very lightly on private matter or correspondence -lrb- 5 -rrb- the letter to he wife be very numerous they be write during he occasional absence from home on business or for relaxation on these occasion he rarely let a day pass without write to he wife and sometimes he write twice on the same day they be full of energy and interest and many extract from they be insert in this history a great deal of the personal history be take from they -lrb- 6 -rrb- all correspondence in any way connect with business during the time that he be astronomer royal be to be find at the royal observatory it be all bind and arrange in the most perfect order and any letter throughout this time can be find with the greatest ease it be very bulky and much of it be in a historical sense very interesting it be no doubt mainly from this correspondence that the autobiography which so far as related to the greenwich part of it be almost entirely a business history be compile the history of the early part of he life be write in great detail and contain a large quantity of family matter which be evidently not intend for publication this part of the autobiography have be compress the history of the latter part of he life be not write by himself at all and have be compile from he journal and other source in both these case and occasionally in short paragraph throughout the narrative it have be find convenient to write the history in the third person 2 the circus greenwich note the syndic of the cambridge university press desire to express they thanks to messr macmillan that follow but it be feel that the present memoir would be incomplete without a reference to those personal characteristic upon which the work of he life hinge and which can only be very faintly gather from he autobiography he be of medium stature and not powerfully build : as he advance in year he stoop a good deal he hand be large-boned and well-formed he constitution be remarkably sound at no period in he life do he seem to have take the least interest in athletic sport or competition but he be a very active pedestrian and could endure a great deal of fatigue he be by no means want in physical courage and on various occasion especially in boat expedition he run considerable risk in debate and controversy he have great self-reliance and be absolutely fearless he eye-sight be peculiar and required correction by spectacles the lens of which be ground to peculiar curve accord to formula which he himself investigate : with these spectacles he see extremely well and he commonly carry three pair adapt to different distance : he take great interest in the change that take place in he eye-sight and write several papers on the subject in he later year he become somewhat deaf but not to the extent of serious personal inconvenience the ruling feature of he character be undoubtedly order from the time that he go up to cambridge to the end of he life he system of order be strictly maintain he write he autobiography up to date soon after he have take he degree and make he first will as soon as he have any money to leave he account be perfectly keep by double entry throughout he life and he value extremely the order of book-keeping : this facility of keep account be very useful to he he seem not to have destroy a document of any kind whatever : counterfoil of old cheque-book note for tradesman circular bill and correspondence of all sort be carefully preserve in the most complete order from the time that he go to cambridge and a huge mass they form to a high appreciation of order he attribute in a great degree he command of mathematics and sometimes speak of mathematics as nothing more than a system of order carry to a considerable extent in everything he be methodical and orderly and he have the greatest dread of disorder creep into the routine work of the observatory even in the smallest matter as a example he spend a whole afternoon in write the word empty on large card to be nail upon a great number of empty packing box because he notice a little confusion arise from they get mixed with other box contain different article and a assistant could not be spare for this work without withdraw he from he appoint duty he arrangement of the observatory correspondence be excellent and elaborate : probably no papers be more easy of reference than those arrange on he system he strict habit of order make he insist very much upon detail in he business with other and the rigid discipline arise out of he system of order make he rule irksome to such of he subordinate as do not conform readily to it : but the efficiency of the observatory unquestionably depend mainly upon it as he power fail with age the ruling passion for order assume a greater prominence and in he last day he seem to be more anxious to put letter which he receive into they proper place for reference than even to master they contents he nature be eminently practical and any subject which have a distinctly practical object and could be advance by mathematical investigation possess interest for he and he dislike of mere theoretical problem and investigation be proportionately great he be continually at war with some of the resident cambridge mathematician on this subject year after year he criticise the senate house papers and the smith 's prize papers question by question very severely : and conduct a interesting and acrimonious private correspondence with professor cayley on the same subject he great mathematical power and he command of mathematics be sufficiently evidence by the numerous mathematical treatise of the highest order which he publish a list of which be append to this biography but a very important feature of he investigation be the thoroughness of they he be never satisfy with leave a result as a barren mathematical expression he would reduce it if possible to a practical and numerical form at any cost of labor : and would use any approximation which would conduce to this result rather than leave the result in a unfruitful condition he never shirk arithmetical work : the longest and most laborious reduction have no terror for he and he be remarkably skilful with the various mathematical expedient for shorten and facilitate arithmetical work of a complex character this power of handle arithmetic be of great value to he in the observatory reduction and in the observatory work generally he regard it as a duty to finish off he work whatever it be and the writer well remember he comment on the mathematics of one of he old friend to the effect that he be too fond of leave a result in the form of three complex equation with three unknown quantity to one who have know in some degree of the enormous quantity of arithmetical work which he have turn out and the unsparing manner in which he have devote himself to it there be something very pathetic in he discovery towards the close of he long life that the figure would not add up he energy and business capacity be remarkable he be make for work and could not long be happy without it whatever subject he be engage upon he keep he object clearly in view and make straight for it aim far more at clearness and directness than at elegance of period or symmetry of arrangement he write he letter with great ease and rapidity : and have write they he very rarely have occasion to re-write they though he often add insertion and interlineation even in the most important official letter without this it would have be impossible for he to have turn out the enormous quantity of correspondence that he do he never dictate letter and only avail himself of clerical assistance in matter of the most ordinary routine in he excursion as in he work he be always energetic and could not endure inaction whatever there be of interest in the place that he visit he examine thoroughly and without delay and then pass on and he thus accomplish a great deal in a short vacation he letter write to he wife while he be on he excursion be very numerous and characteristic and afford ample proof of he incessant energy and activity both of body and mind they be not brilliantly write for it be not in he nature to write for effect and he would never give himself the trouble to study the composition of he letter but they be straight-forward clear and concise and he be never at a loss for suitable language to express he idea he have a wonderful capacity for enjoyment : the subject that chiefly interest he be scenery architecture and antiquity but everything novel or curious have a interest for he he make several journey to the continent but by far the greater number of he excursion be make in england and scotland and there be few part of the country which he have not visit he be very fond of the lake district of cumberland and visit it very frequently and each time that he go there the same set of view have a eternal freshness for he and he write long description of the scenery and effect with the same rapture as if he have see it for the first time many of he letter be write from playford a village in a beautiful part of suffolk a few mile from ipswich here he have a small property and generally stay there for a short time once or twice a year he be extremely fond of this country and be never tire of repeat he walk by the well-known lane and footpath and as in cumberland the suffolk country have a eternal freshness and novelty for he wherever he go he be indefatigable in keep up he acquaintance with he numerous friend and he letter abound in social reminiscence he memory be singularly retentive it be much remark at school in he early day and in the course of he life he have store up in he memory a incredible quantity of poetry ballad and miscellaneous fact and information of all sort which be all constantly ready and at he service it be almost needless to add that he memory be equally accurate and extensive in matter connect with science or business he independence of character be no doubt due to and inseparable from he great power the value of he scientific work greatly depend upon he self-reliance and independence of thought and in the heavy work of remodel the observatory it be a very valuable quality this same self-reliance make he in he latter year apt to draw conclusion too confidently and hastily on subject which he have take up more as a pastime than as work but whatever he touch he deal with ably and in the most fearless truthseeking manner and leave original and vigorous opinion he have a remarkably well-balanced mind and a simplicity of nature that appear invulnerable no amount of hero-worship seem to have the least effect upon he and from a very early time he be expose to a great deal of it he mind be incessantly engage on investigation of nature and this seem to have be with he as have be the case with other a preserving influence this simplicity of character he retain throughout he life at the same time he be sensible and shrewd in he money matter and attentive to he personal interest and he practical good sense in the general affair of life combine with he calm and steady consideration of point submit to he make he advice very valuable this be especially recognize by he own and he wife 's relation who consult he on many occasion and place the fullest confidence in he absolute sense of justice as well as in he wise counsel he be extremely liberal in proportion to he means and give away money to a large extent to all who have any claim upon he but he be not in any sense reckless and keep a most cautious eye on he expense he be not indifferent to the honor which he receive in the scientific world but he do not appear to have seek they in any way and he certainly do not trouble himself about they he courtesy be unfailing : no amount of trouble could shake it whether it be the secretary of the admiralty or a servant girl want she fortune tell : whether a begging-letter for money or miscellaneous invitation : all have they answer in the most clear and courteous language but he would not grant personal interview when he could avoid it : they take up too much of he time he head be so clear that he never seem to want for the clearest and most direct language in express he meaning and he letter be model of terseness in all he view and opinion he be strongly liberal at cambridge at a early date he be one of the 83 member of the senate who support the application to permit the grant of medical degree without require a expression of assent to the religious doctrine of the church of england and in 1868 he decline to sign a petition against the abolition of religious declaration require of person admit to fellowship or proceed to the degree of ma and he be oppose to every kind of narrowness and exclusiveness when he be appoint to the post of astronomer royal he stipulate that he should not be ask to vote in any political election but all he view be in the liberal direction he be a great reader of theology and church history and as regarded form of worship and the interpretation of the scripture he treat they with great respect but from the point of view of a freethinking layman in the preface to he note on the earlier hebrew scripture he say in regard to the general tone of these note i will first remark that i have nothing to say on the subject of verbal inspiration with those who entertain that doctrine i can have nothing in common nor do i recognize in the professedly historical account any other inspiration which can exempt they from the severest criticism that would be applicable to so-called profane account write under the same general circumstance and in the same country and he treatment of the subject in the note shew how entirely he take a rationalistic view of the whole question he also strongly side with bishop colenso in he fearless criticism of the pentateuch though he dissent from some of he conclusion but he be deeply imbue with the spirit of religion and reflect much upon it he whole correspondence convey the impression of the most sterling integrity and high-mindedness without a trace of affectation in no letter do there appear a shadow of waver on matter of principle whether in public or private matter and he be very clear and positive in he conviction the great secret of he long and successful official career be that he be a good servant and thoroughly understand he position he never set himself in opposition to he master the admiralty he never hesitate to ask the admiralty for what he think right whether in the way of money grant for various object or for occasional permission to give he service to scientific matter not immediately connect with the observatory sometimes the admiralty refuse he request and he feel this very keenly but he be far too busy and energetic to trouble himself about such little slight and cheerfully accept the situation what be refuse by one administration be frequently grant by another and in the meantime he be always ready to give he most zealous assistance in any matter that be officially bring before he this cheerful readiness to help combine with he great ability and punctuality in business matter make he a very valuable servant and speak generally he have the confidence of the admiralty in a remarkable degree in many of he report to the board of visitor he speak gratefully of the liberality of the admiralty in forwarding scientific progress and research in matter too which be perhaps of minor importance from the high stand-point of science but which be invaluable in the conduct of a important business office such for example as estimate and official correspondence he be orderly and punctual in the highest degree and what be by no means unimportant he possess a excellent official style in correspondence combine with great clearness of expression he entire honesty of purpose and the high respect in which he be hold both at home and abroad give great weight to he recommendation with regard to he habit while he reside at the observatory he custom be to work in he official room from 9 to about 230 though in summer he be frequently at work before breakfast he then take a brisk walk and dine at about 330 this early hour have be prescribe and insist upon by he physician dr haviland of cambridge in whom he have great confidence he eat heartily though simply and moderately and sleep for about a hour after dinner he then have tea and from about 7 to 10 he work in the same room with he family he would never retire to a private room and regard the society of he family as highly beneficial in take the edge off he work he power of abstraction be remarkable : nothing seem to disturb he neither music singing nor miscellaneous conversation he would then play a game or two at card read a few page of a classical or historical book and retire at 11 on sunday he attend morning service at church and in the evening read a few prayer very carefully and impressively to he whole household he be very hospitable and delighted to receive he friend in a simple and natural way at he house in this he be most admirably aid by he wife whose grace and skill make everything pleasant to they guest but he avoid dinner-party as much as possible -- they interfere too much with he work -- and with the exception of scientific and official dinner he seldom dine away from home he taste be entirely domestic and he be very happy in he family with he natural love of work and with the incessant call upon he he would soon have break down have it not be for he system of regular relaxation two or three time a year he take a holiday : generally a short run of a week or ten day in the spring a trip of a month or thereabout in the early autumn and about three week at playford in the winter these trip be always conduct in the most active manner either in constant motion from place to place or in daily active excursion this system he maintain with great regularity and from the exceed interest and enjoyment that he take in these trip he mind be so much refresh and steady that he always keep himself equal to he work airy seem to have have a strong bent in the direction of astronomy from he youth and it be curious to note how well furnished he be by the time that he become astronomer royal both with astronomy in all its branch and with the kindred science so necessary for the practical working and improvement of it at the time that he go to cambridge physical astronomy be greatly study there and form a most important part of the university course he eagerly avail himself of this and master the physical astronomy in the most thorough manner as be evidence by he papers collect in he mathematical tract he investigation of the long inequality of the earth and venus and many other work as plumian professor he have charge of the small observatory at cambridge where he do a great deal of the observe and reduction work himself and become thoroughly verse in the practical working of a observatory the result of this be immediately see in the improve method which he introduce at greenwich and which be speedily imitate at other observatory optics and the undulatory theory of light have be very favorite subject with he and he have write and lecture frequently upon they in the construction of the new and powerful telescope and other optical instrument require from time to time this knowledge be very essential for in its instrumental equipment the greenwich observatory be entirely remodel during he tenure of office and in many of the matter refer to he as for instance that of the lighthouse a thorough knowledge of optics be most valuable he have make a great study of the theory and construction of clock and this knowledge be invaluable to he at greenwich in the establishment of new and more accurate astronomical clock and especially in the improvement of chronometer he have carefully study the theory of pendulum and have learn how to use they in he experiment in the cornish mine this knowledge he afterwards utilize very effectively at the harton pit in compare the density of the earth 's crust with its mean density and it be very useful to he in connection with geodetic survey and experiment on which he be consult and he mechanical knowledge be useful in almost everything the subject -lrb- outside those require for he professional work -rrb- in which he take most interest be poetry history theology antiquity architecture and engineering he be well acquaint with standard english poetry and have commit large quantity to memory which he frequently refer to as a most valuable acquisition and a ever-present relief and comfort to he mind history and theology he have study as opportunity offer and without be widely read in they he be much at home with they and he powerful memory make the most of what he do read antiquity and architecture be very favorite subject with he he have visit most of the camp and castle in the unite kingdom and be never tire of trace they connection with ancient military event : and he write several papers on this subject especially those relate to the roman invasion of britain ecclesiastical architecture he be very fond of : he have visit nearly all the cathedral and principal church in england and many on the continent and be most enthusiastic on they different style and merit : he letter abound in critical remark on they he be extremely well verse in mechanic and in the principle and theory of construction and take the greatest interest in large engineering work this lead to much communication with stephenson brunel and other engineer who consult he freely on the subject of great work on which they be engage : in particular he render much assistance in connection with the construction of the britannium bridge over the menai strait there be various other subject which he read with much interest -lrb- geology in particular -rrb- but he make no study of natural history and know very little about it beyond detach fact he industry be untiring and in go over he book one by one it be very noticeable how large a number of they be feathered with he paper mark shew how carefully he have read they and refer to they he nature be essentially cheerful and literature of a witty and humourous character have a great charm for he he be very fond of music and know a great number of song and he be well acquaint with the theory of music : but he be no performer he do not sketch freehand but make excellent drawing with he camera lucida at the time when he take he degree -lrb- 1823 -rrb- and for many year afterwards there be very great activity of scientific investigation and astronomical enterprise in england and as in the time of flamsteed and halley the earnest zeal of man of science occasionally lead to much controversy and bitterness amongst they airy be by no means exempt from such controversy he be a man of keen sensitiveness though it be combine with great steadiness of temper and he never hesitate to attack theory and method that he consider to be scientifically wrong this lead to difference with ivory challi south cayley archibald smith and other but however much he might differ from they he be always personally courteous and the dispute generally go no farther than as regard the special matter in question almost all these controversial discussion be carry on openly and be publish in the athenaeum the philosophical magazine or elsewhere for he print nearly everything that he write and be very careful in the selection of the most suitable channel for publication he regard it as a duty to popularize as much as possible the work do at the observatory and to take the public into he confidence and this he effect by article communicate to newspaper lecture numerous papers write for scientific society report debate and critique he strong constitution and he regular habit both of work and exercise be sufficient explanation of the good health which in general he enjoy not but what he have sharp touch of illness from time to time at one period he suffer a good deal from a attack of eczema and at another from a varicose vein in he leg and he be occasionally trouble with severe cold but he bear these ailment with great patience and throw they off in course of time he be happy in he marriage and in he family and such trouble and distress as be inevitable he accept calmly and quietly in he death as in he life he be fortunate : he have no long or painful illness and he be spare the calamity of aberration of intellect the saddest of all visitation chapter ii from he birth to he take he ba degree at cambridge from July 27th 1801 to January 18th 1823 george biddell airy be bear at alnwick in northumberland on July 27th 1801 he father be william airy of luddington in lincolnshire the descendant of a long line of airy who have be trace back with a very high degree of probability to a family of that name which be settle at kentmere in westmorland in the 14th century a branch of this family migrate to pontefract in yorkshire where they seem to have prosper for many year but they be involve in the consequence of the civil war and one member of the family retire to ousefleet in yorkshire he grandson remove to luddington in lincolnshire where he descendant for several generation pursue the calling of small farmer george biddell airy 's mother ann airy be the daughter of george biddell a well-to-do farmer in suffolk william airy the father of george biddell airy be a man of great activity and strength and of prudent and steady character when a young man he become foreman on a farm in the neighborhood of luddington and lay by he earnings in summer in order to educate himself in winter for a person in he rank he education be unusually good in matter of science and in english literature but at the age of 24 he grow tired of country labor and obtain a post in the excise after serve in various collection he be appoint collector of the northumberland collection on the 15th August 1800 and during he service there he eldest son george biddell airy be bear the time over which he service as officer and supervisor extend be that in which smuggling rise to a very high pitch and in which the position of excise officer be sometimes dangerous he be remarkable for he activity and boldness in contest with smuggler and make many seizure ann airy the mother of george biddell airy be a woman of great natural ability both speculative and practical kind as a neighbor and as head of a family and be deeply love and respect the family consist of george biddell elizabeth william and arthur who die young william airy be appoint to hereford collection on 22nd October 1802 and remove thither shortly after he stay at hereford till he be appoint to essex collection on 28th February 1810 and during this time george biddell be educate at elementary school in write arithmetic and a little latin he record of himself that he be not a favorite with the schoolboy for he have very little animal vivacity and seldom join in active play with he schoolfellow but in the proceedings of the school he be successful and be a favorite with he master on the appointment of william airy to essex collection the family remove to colchester on April 5th 1810 here george biddell be first send to a large school in sir isaac 's walk then keep by mr byatt walker and be soon note for he correctness in orthography geography and arithmetic he evidently make rapid progress for on one occasion mr walker say openly in the schoolroom how remarkable it be that a boy 10 year old should be the first in the school at this school he stay till the end of 1813 and thoroughly learn arithmetic -lrb- from walkingame 's book -rrb- book-keeping by double entry -lrb- on which knowledge throughout he life he set a special value -rrb- the use of the slide rule -lrb- which knowledge also be specially useful to he in after life -rrb- mensuration and algebra -lrb- from bonnycastle 's book -rrb- he also study grammar in all its branch and geography and acquire some knowledge of english literature begin with that admirable book the speaker but it do not appear that latin and greek be attend to at this school he record that at this time he learn a infinity of snatch of song small romance & c which he powerful memory retain most accurately throughout he life he be no hand at active play : but be notorious for he skill in construct gun for shooting pea and arrow and other mechanical contrivance at home he relate that he pick up a wonderful quantity of learn from he father 's book he read and remember much poetry from such standard author as milton pope gay gray swift & c which be destine to prove in after life a invaluable relaxation for he mind but he also study deeply a excellent cyclopaedia call a dictionary of art and science in three volume folio and learn from it much about ship-building navigation fortification and many other subject during this period he valuable friendship with he uncle arthur biddell commence arthur biddell be a prosperous farmer and valuer at playford near ipswich he be a well-informed and able man of powerful and original mind extremely kind and good-natured and greatly respect throughout the county in the autobiography of george biddell airy he state as follow : i do not remember precisely when it be that i first visit my uncle arthur biddell i think it be in a winter : certainly as early as the winter of 1812 -- 13 here i find a friend whose society i could enjoy and i entirely appreciate and enjoy the practical mechanical and at the same time speculative and enquire talent of arthur biddell he have a library which for a person in middle life may be call excellent and he historical and antiquarian knowledge be not small after spend one winter holiday with he it easily come to pass that i spend the next summer holiday with he : and at the next winter holiday finding that there be no precise arrangement for my movement i secretly write he a letter beg he to come with a gig to fetch I home with he : he comply with my request give no hint to my father or mother of my letter : and from that time one-third of every year be regularly spend with he till i go to college how great be the influence of this on my character and education i can not tell it be with he that i become acquaint with the messr ransome w cubitt the civil engineer -lrb- afterwards sir w cubitt -rrb- bernard barton thoma clarkson -lrb- the slave-trade abolitionist -rrb- and other person whose acquaintance i have value highly it be also with he that i become acquaint with the work of the best modern poet scott byron campbell hogg and other : as also with the waverley novel and other work of merit in 1813 william airy lose he appointment of collector of excise and be in consequence very much straiten in he circumstance but there be no relaxation in the education of he child and at the beginning of 1814 george biddell be send to the endowed grammar school at colchester then keep by the rev e crosse and remain there till the summer of 1819 when he go to college the autobiography proceed as follow : i become here a respectable scholar in latin and greek to the extent of accurate translation and composition of prose latin : in regard to latin verse i be i think more defective than most scholar who take the same pain but i be not much ashamed of this for i entirely despise the system of instruction in verse composition my father on some occasion have to go to london and bring back for I a pair of 12-inch globe they be invaluable to I the first star which i learn from the celestial globe be alpha lyra alpha aquila alpha cygnus : and to this time i involuntarily regard these star as the birth-star of my astronomical knowledge have somewhere see a description of a gunter 's quadrant i perceive that i could construct one by means of the globe : my father procure for I a board of the proper shape with paper paste on it and on this i trace the line of the quadrant my command of geometry be tolerably complete and one way in which i frequently amuse myself be by make paper model -lrb- most carefully draw in outline -rrb- which be button together without any cement or sewing thus i make model not only of regular solid regularly irregular solid cone cut in all direction so as to shew the conic section and the like but also of six-gun battery intrenchment and fortress of various kind & c from various book i have learn the construction of the steam-engine : the older form from the dictionary of art and science newer form from modern book the newest form however -lrb- with the slide steam valve -rrb- i learn from a 6-horse engine at bawtrey 's brewery -lrb- in which mr keel the father of my schoolfellow have acquire a partnership -rrb- i frequently go to look at this engine and on one occasion have the extreme felicity of examine some of its part when it be open for repair in the mean time my education be advance at playford the first record i believe which i have of my attention to mechanic there be the plan of a threshing-machine which i draw but i be acquire valuable information of all kind from the encyclopedia londinensis a work which without be high in any respect be one of the most generally useful that i have see but i well remember one of the most important step that i ever make i have try experiment with the object-glass of a opera-glass and be greatly astonish at the appearance of the image of object see through the glass under different condition by these thing my thought be turn to accurate optics and i read with care rutherford 's lecture which my uncle possess the acquisition of a accurate knowledge of the effect of optical construction be one of the most charming attainment that i ever reach long before i go to college i understand the action of the lens of a telescope better than most optician i also read with great zeal nicholson 's dictionary of chemistry and occasionally make chemical experiment of a inexpensive kind : indeed i grow so fond of this subject that there be some thought of apprentice I to a chemist i also attend to survey and make a tolerable survey and map of my uncle 's farm at school i be go on successfully and distinguish myself particularly by my memory it be the custom for each boy once a week to repeat a number of line of latin or greek poetry the number depend very much on he own choice i determine on repeat 100 every week and i never once fall below that number and be sometimes much above it it be no distress to I and great enjoyment at michaelma 1816 i repeat 2394 line probably without miss a word i do not think that i be a favorite with mr crosse but he certainly have a high opinion of my power and express this to my father my father entertain the idea of send I to college which mr crosse recommend : but he hear from some college man that the expense would be _ # 200 _ a year and he lay aside all thought of it the farm of playford hall be in 1813 or 1814 hire by thoma clarkson the slave-trade abolitionist my uncle transact much business for he -lrb- as a neighbor and friend -rrb- in the management of the farm & c for a time and they become very intimate my uncle beg he to examine I in classical knowledge and he do so i think twice he also give some better information about the probable expense & c at college the result be a strong recommendation by my uncle or through my uncle that i should be send to cambridge and this be adopt by my father i think it likely that this be in 1816 in December 1816 dealtry 's fluxion be buy for I and i read it and understand it well i borrow hutton 's course of mathematics of old mr ransome who have come to reside at greenstead near colchester and read a good deal of it about ladyday 1817 i begin to read mathematics with mr roger -lrb- formerly i think a fellow of sidney college and a indifferent mathematician of the cambridge school -rrb- who have succeed a mr tweed as assistant to mr crosse in the school i go to he house twice a week on holiday afternoon i do not remember how long i receive lesson from he but i think to June 1818 this course be extremely valuable to I not on account of mr roger 's ability -lrb- for i understand many thing better than he do -rrb- but for its training I both in cambridge subject and in the cambridge accurate method of treat they i go through euclid -lrb- as far as usually read -rrb- wood 's algebra wood 's mechanic vince 's hydrostatic wood 's optics trigonometry -lrb- in a geometrical treatise and also in woodhouse 's algebraical form -rrb- fluxion to a good extent newton 's principium to the end of the 9th section this be a large quantity but i read it accurately and understand it perfectly and could write out any one of the proposition which i have read in the most exact form my connexion with mr roger be terminate by _ he _ give I notice that he could not undertake to receive I any longer : in fact i be too much for he i generally read these book in a garret in we house in george lane which be indefinitely appropriate to my brother and myself i find that i copy out vince 's conic section in February 1819 the first book that i copy be the small geometrical treatise on trigonometry in may 1817 : to this i be urge by old mr ransome upon my complain that i could not purchase the book : and it be no bad lesson of independence to I during the same period 1817-1819 he be occupy at school on translation into blank verse from the aeneid and iliad and read through the whole of sophocle very carefully the classical knowledge which he thus gain at school and subsequently at cambridge be sound and he take great pleasure in it : throughout he life he make a practice of keep one or other of the classical author at hand for occasional relaxation he terminate he schooling in June 1819 shortly afterwards he father leave colchester and go to reside at bury st edmund 's the autobiography proceed as follow : mr clarkson be at one time inclined to recommend I to go to st peter 's college -lrb- which have be much enrich by a bequest from a mr gisborne -rrb- but on give some account of I to he friend mr jame d hustler tutor of trinity college mr hustler urge upon he that i be exactly the proper sort of person to go to trinity college and thus it be settle -lrb- mainly by mr clarkson -rrb- that i should be enter at trinity college i think that i be send for purposely from colchester to playford and on march 6th 1819 i ride in company with mr clarkson from playford to sproughton near ipswich to be examine by the rev mr roger incumbent of sproughton a old ma of trinity college and be examine and my certificate duly send to mr hustler and i be enter on mr hustler 's side as sizar of trinity college in the summer of 1819 i spend some time at playford on July 27th 1819 -lrb- my birthday 18 year old -rrb- mr clarkson invite I to dinner to meet mr charle musgrave fellow of trinity college who be reside for a short time at grundisburgh take the church duty there for dr ramsden the rector it be arrange that i should go to grundisburgh the next day -lrb- i think -rrb- to be examine in mathematics by mr musgrave i go accordingly and mr musgrave set before I a paper of question in geometry algebra mechanic optics & c end with the first proposition of the principium i know nothing more about my answer at the time but i find long after that they excite so much admiration that they be transmit to cambridge -lrb- i forget whether to mr musgrave 's brother a fellow of trinity college and afterwards archbishop of york or to mr peacock afterwards dean of ely -rrb- and be long preserve the list of the classical subject for the first year in trinity college be transmit to I as usual by mr hustler they be -- the hippolytus of euripide the 3rd book of thucydide and the 2nd philippic of cicero these i read carefully and note before go up mr hustler 's family live in bury and i call on he and see he in October introduce by mr clarkson on the morning of October 18th 1819 i go on the top of the coach to cambridge know nobody there but mr hustler but have letter of introduction from mr charle musgrave to professor sedgwick mr thoma musgrave and mr george peacock all fellow of trinity college i be set down at the hoop see trinity college for the first time find mr hustler be conduct by he servant to the robe-maker 's where i be invest in the cap and blue gown and after some further waiting be install into lodgings in bridge street at 4 o'clock i go to the college hall and be introduce by mr hustler to several undergraduate generally clever man and in the evening i attend chapel in my surplice -lrb- it be st luke 's day -rrb- and witness that splendid service of which the occasional exhibition well befit the place as soon as possible i call on mr peacock mr musgrave and professor sedgwick by all i be receive with great kindness : my examination papers have be send to they and a considerable reputation precede I mr peacock at once desire that i would not consider mr c musgrave 's letter as a ordinary introduction but that i would refer to he on all occasion and i do so for several year and always receive from he the greatest assistance that he could give i think that i do not become acquaint with mr whewell till the next term when i meet he at a breakfast party at mr peacock 's mr peacock at once warn I to arrange for take regular exercise and prescribe a walk of two hour every day before dinner : a rule to which i attend regularly and to which i ascribe the continuance of good general health i shewed mr peacock a manuscript book which contain a number of original proposition which i have investigate these much increase my reputation -lrb- i really have sense enough to set no particular value on it -rrb- and i be soon know by sight to almost everybody in the university a ridiculous little circumstance aid in this the former rule of the university -lrb- strictly enforce -rrb- have be that all student should wear drab knee-breeches : and i at mr clarkson 's recommendation be so fit up the struggle between the old dress and the trowser customary in society be still go on but almost terminate and i be one of the very few freshman who retain the old habiliment this make I in some measure distinguishable : however at the end of my first three term i lay these aside the college lecture begin on oct 22 : mr evan at 9 on the hippolytus and mr peacock at 10 on euclid -lrb- these be the assistant tutor on mr hustler 's side -rrb- : and then i feel myself establish i write in a day or two to my uncle arthur biddell and i receive from he a letter of the utmost kindness he enter gravely on the consideration of my prospects my want & c : and offer at all time to furnish I with money which he think my father 's parsimonious habit might make he unwilling to do i never have occasion to avail myself of this offer : but it be make in a way which in no small degree strengthen the kindly feeling that have long exist between we i carefully attend the lecture take note as appear necessary in mathematics there be geometrical problem algebra trigonometry -lrb- which latter subject the lecture do not reach till the term of 1820 -rrb- mr peacock give I a copy of lacroix 's differential calculus as translate by himself and herschel and babbage and also a copy of they example at this time the use of differential calculus be just prevail over that of fluxion -lrb- which i have learn -rrb- i betook myself to it with great industry i also make myself master of the theory of rectangular coordinate and some of the differential process apply to they which only a few of the best of the university mathematician then wholly possess in classical subject i read the latin -lrb- seneca 's -rrb- and english hippolytus racine 's phdre -lrb- which my sister translate for I -rrb- and all other book to which i be I to say that one of the company of fishmonger mr r sharp have give to mr john h smyth mp for norwich the presentation to a small exhibition of _ # 20 _ a year which mr smyth have place in mr hustler 's hand and which mr hustler immediately confer on I this be my first step towards pecuniary independence i retain this exhibition till i become a fellow of the college i stay at cambridge during part of the winter vacation and to avoid expense i quit my lodgings and go for a time into somebody 's room in the bishop 's hostel -lrb- it be customary for the tutor to place student in room when they right owner be absent -rrb- i take with I thucydide and all relate to it and read the book upon which the next term 's lecture be to be found very carefully the latter part of the vacation i spend at bury where i begin with the assistance of my sister to pick up a little french : as i perceive that it be absolutely necessary for enable I to read modern mathematics during a part of the time i employ myself in write out a paper on the geometrical interpretation of the algebraical expression sqrt -lrb- -1 -rrb- i think that the original suggestion of perpendicular line come from some book -lrb- i do not remember clearly -rrb- and i work it out in several instance pretty well especially in de moivre 's theorem i have speak of it in the precede term to mr peacock and he encourage I to to cambridge take up my abode in my former lodgings i shewed my paper on sqrt -lrb- -1 -rrb- to mr peacock who be much pleased with it and shew it to mr whewell and other on February 1 i commence two excellent custom the first be that i always have upon my table a quire of large-sized scribbling-paper sew together : and upon this paper everything be enter : translation into latin and out of greek mathematical problem memorandum of every kind -lrb- the latter transfer when necessary to the subsequent page -rrb- and generally with the date of the day this be a most valuable custom the other be this : as i perceive that to write latin prose well would be useful to I i write a translation of english into latin every day however much press i might be with other business i endeavor to write at least three or four word but if possible i write a good many sentence i may fix upon this as the time when my daily habit be settle in the form in which they continue for several year i rise in time for the chapel service at 7 it be the college regulation that every student should attend chapel four morning and four evening -lrb- Sunday be one of each -rrb- in every week : and in this i never fail after chapel service i come to my lodgings and breakfast at 9 i go to college lecture which last to 11 most of my contemporary be intend for the church attend also divinity lecture : but i never do i then return put my lecture note in order write my piece of latin prose and then employ myself on the subject which i be read for the time : usually take mathematics at this hour at 2 or a little sooner i go out for a long walk usually 4 or 5 mile into the country : sometimes if i find companion i row on the cam -lrb- a practice acquire rather later -rrb- a little before 4 i return and at 4 go to college hall after dinner i lounge till evening chapel time 1\/2 past 5 and return about 6 i then have tea then i read quietly usually a classical subject till 11 and i never even in the time when i might seem most severely press sit up later from this time to the close of the annual examination -lrb- beginning of June -rrb- i remain at cambridge stop there through the easter vacation the subject of the mathematical lecture be ordinary algebra and trigonometry : but mr peacock always have some private problem of a higher class for I and see I i believe every day the subject of the classical lecture be the termination of hippolytus the book of thucydide and the oration of cicero in mathematics i read whewell 's mechanic then just publish -lrb- the first innovation make in the cambridge system of physical science for many year -rrb- : and i find in my scribbling-paper note integral central force finite difference steam-engine construction and power plan of bridge spherical college authority on Wednesday may 24th 1820 the examination begin i be anxious about the result of the examination but only in such a degree as to make my conduct perfectly steady and calm and to mr hustler to be lodge in room in college : and be put first into room in bishop 's hostel and subsequently into room in the great court the first affair that i have in college be one of standard classic -lrb- unconnected with the lecture -rrb- and at 11 precisely i leave off and go to bed i continue my daily translation into latin prose as before on August 24th 1820 rosser a man of 's medal -lrb- classical prize -rrb- but he now seem to be cool in he advice and i lay aside all thought of it \* \* \* \* \* it seem not out of place to insert here a copy of some cambridge reminiscence write by airy which will serve to explain the act and opponency refer to in the previous narrative and other matter the act the examination for ba degree be precede in my time by keep two act in the school under the university library : the second of they in the October term immediately before the examination the first -lrb- i think -rrb- in the October term of the preceding year these act be relique of the disputation of the middle age which probably hold a very important place in the discipline of the university -lrb- there seem to be something like they in some of the continental university -rrb- the preside authority be one of the moderator i apprehend that the word moderator signify president in which sense it be still use in the kirk of scotland and that it be peculiarly apply to the presidency of the disputation the most important educational arrangement in the university the moderator send a summons to the respondent to submit three subject for argument and to prepare to defend they on a give day : he also name three opponent this and all the follow proceedings be conduct in latin for my act of 1822 nov 6 i submit the following subject : recte statuit newtonus in principii sui mathematici libro primo sectione undecim recte statuit woodius de iride recte statuit paleius de obligationibus the opponent name to attack these assertion be hamilton of st john 's rusby of st catharine 's field of trinity it be customary for the opponent to meet at tea at the room of the senior opponent in order to discuss and arrange they argument the respondent be also invite but he be warn that he must depart as soon as tea would be finish : then the three opponent proceed with they occupation as i have act in both capacity i be able to say that the matter be transact in a earnest and business-like way indeed in the time precede my own -lrb- i know not whether in my own time -rrb- the assistance of a private tutor be frequently engage and i remember hear a senior ma remark that my college tutor -lrb- jame d hustler -rrb- be the best crammer for a act in the university at the appoint time the party meet in the school : the respondent first read a latin thesis on any subject -lrb- i think i take some metaphysical subject -rrb- but nobody pay any attention to it : then the respondent read he first dogma and the first opponent produce a argument against it in latin after this there be repeat reply and rejoinder all in viv voce latin the moderator sometimes interpose a remark in latin when he consider that one argument be dispose of he call for another by the word probe aliter the argument be sometimes shape with considerable ingenuity and require a clear head in the respondent when all be finish the moderator make a complimentary remark to the respondent and one to the first opponent -lrb- i forget whether to the second and third -rrb- in my respondency of 1822 November 6 the compliment be quaestiones tua summo ingenio et acumine defendisti et in rebus mathematici scientiam plan mirabilem ostendistus in a opponency -lrb- i forget when -rrb- the compliment be magno ingenio argumenta tua et construxisti et defendisti the act of the high man excite much interest among the student at my act the room be crowd with undergraduate i imagine that at a time somewhat distant the maintenance of the act be the only regulation by which the university act on the study of the place when the act have be properly keep license be give to the father of the college to present the undergraduate to the vice-chancellor who then solemnly admit he ad respondendum quaestionus there be no appearance of collective examination before this presentation : what the quaestio might be i do not know still the undergraduate be not ba the quaestio however be finish and approve before the day of a certain congregation and then the undergraduate be declare to be actualiter in artibus baccalaureum probably these regulation be find to be insufficient for the control of education and the January examination be institute i conjecture this to have be at or shortly before the date of the earliest tripos record in the cambridge calendar 1748 the increase importance of the January examination naturally diminish the value of the act in the eye of the undergraduate and a few year after my ma degree it be find that the opponent meet not for the purpose of conceal they argument from the respondent but for the purpose of reveal they to he this lead to the entire suppression of the system the most active man in this suppression be mr whewell : its date must have be near to 1830 the shape in which the argument be deliver by a opponent reading from a write paper be si -lrb- quote something from the respondent 's challenge -rrb- & c & c cadit quaestio sed -lrb- cite something else bear on the subject of discussion -rrb- valet consequentium ergo -lrb- combine these to prove some inaccuracy in the respondent 's challenge -rrb- valent consequentium et argumentum nobody pretend to understand these mystical termination apparently the original idea be that several act should be keep by each undergraduate for to keep up the number -lrb- as it seem -rrb- each student have to gabble through a ridiculous form si quaestiones tuae falsae sint cadit quaestio : -- sed quaestione tuae falsae sunt ergo valent consequentium et argumentum i have forget time and place when this be utter the senate-house examination the questionist as the undergraduate prepare for ba be call in the October term be consider as a separate body collect at a separate table in hall attend no lecture but invite to attend a system of trial examination conduct by one of the tutor or assistant-tutor from the act from the annual college examination and -lrb- i suppose -rrb- from enquiry in the separate college the moderator acquire a general idea of the relative merit of the candidate for honor guide by this the candidate be divide into six class the moderator and assistant examiner be provide each with a set of question in manuscript -lrb- no print papers be use for honor in the senate house in regard to the -lrb- greek : hous pollous -rrb- i can not say -rrb- on the Monday on which the examination begin the father of the college receive all the questionist -lrb- i believe -rrb- at any rate all the candidate for honor at breakfast in the combination room at 8 o'clock and march they to the senate house my place with other honour-man be in the east gallery there one examiner take charge of the 1st and 2nd class unite another examiner take the 3rd and 4th class unite and a third take the 5th and 6th unite on Tuesday one examiner take the 1st class alone a second take the 2nd and 3rd class unite a third take the 4th and 5th class unite and a fourth take the 6th class alone on Wednesday Thursday and Friday the change be similar and in all the question be thus conduct the examiner read from he manuscript the first question those who could answer it proceed to write out they answer and as soon as one have finish he give the word do then the examiner read out he second question repeat it when necessary for the understanding by those who take it up more lately and so on i think that the same process be repeat in the afternoon but i do not remember precisely in this manner the examination be conduct through five day -lrb- Monday to Friday -rrb- with no interruption except on Friday afternoon it be principally perhaps entirely bookwork but on two _ evening _ there be print papers of problem : and the examination in these be conduct just as in the print papers of the present day : but in the private college room of the moderator and there wine and other refreshment be offer to the examinee how this singular custom begin i know not the order of merit be work out on Friday afternoon and evening and be in some measure know through the university late in the evening i remember mr peacock come to a party of examinee and give information on several place i do not remember he mention mine -lrb- though undoubtedly he do -rrb- but i distinctly remember he give the wooden spoon on the Saturday morning at 8 o'clock the manuscript list be nail to the door of the senate-house the form of further proceedings in the presentation for degree -lrb- ad respondendum quaestionus -rrb- i imagine have not be much alter the kneel before the vice-chancellor and place hand in the vice-chancellor 's hand be those of the old form of do homage the form of examination which i have describe be complicate and perhaps troublesome but i believe that it be very efficient possibly more so than the modern form -lrb- established i suppose at the same time as the abolition of the act -rrb- the proportion of question now answer to the whole number set be ridiculously small and no accurate idea of relative merit can be form from they the college hall when i go up in 1819 and for several year later the dinner be at 1\/4 past 3 there be no supplementary dinner for special demand boat-club i think be not invent even in a plain social way till about 1824 or 1825 and not in connection with the college till some year later some of the senior fellow speak of the time when dinner be at 2 and regret the change there be supper in hall at 9 o'clock : i have know it to be attend by a few undergraduate when tire by examination or by evening walk and there be always some senior at the upper table : i have occasionally join they and have have some very interesting conversation the supper be cold but hot addition be make when require one little arrangement amuse I as shew the ecclesiastical character of the college the fast of the church be to be strictly keep and there be to be no dinner in hall it be thus arrange the evening chapel service which be usually at 5-1\/2 -lrb- i think -rrb- be hold at 3 and at 4 the ordinary full meal be serve in hall but as it follow the chapel attendance it be hold to be supper and there be no subsequent meal there be no chair whatever in hall except the single chair of the vice-master at the head of the table on the dais and that of the senior dean at the table next the east wall all other sit on bench and i have hear allusion to a ludicrous difficulty which occur when some princess -lrb- of the royal family -rrb- dine in the hall and it be a great puzzle how to get they to the right side of the bench the sizar dine after all the rest they dinner usually begin soon after 4 for the non-foundationist a separate dinner be provide as for pensioner but for the foundationist the remains of the fellow ' dinner be bring down and i think that this provision be generally prefer to the other the dish at all the table of undergraduate be of pewter till a certain day when they be change for porcelain i can not remember whether this be at the time when they become questionist -lrb- in the October term -rrb- or at the time when they be declare actualiter esse in artibus baccalaureo -lrb- in the lend term -rrb- up to the questionist time the undergraduate scholar have no mixture whatever they be the only pure table in the hall : and i look on this as a matter very valuable for the ultimate state of the college society but in the October term those who be to proceed to ba be draft into the mixed body of questionist : and they greatly dislike the change they continue so till the lend term when they be formally invite by the bachelor scholar to join the upper table mathematical subject of study and examination in the October term 1819 the only book on pure mathematics be : -- euclid generally algebra by dr wood -lrb- formerly tutor but in 1819 master of st john 's college -rrb- vince 's fluxion and dealtry 's fluxion woodhouse 's and other trigonometry not a whisper pass through the university generally on the subject of differential calculus although some papers -lrb- subsequently much value -rrb- on that subject have be write by mr woodhouse fellow of caius college but they style be repulsive and they never take hold of the university whewell 's mechanic -lrb- 1819 -rrb- contain a few and easy application of the differential calculus the book on apply mathematics be wood 's mechanic whewell 's mechanic wood 's optics vince 's hydrostatic vince 's astronomy woodhouse 's plane astronomy -lrb- perhaps rather later -rrb- the first book of newton 's principium : i do not remember any other these work be undoubtedly able and for the great proportion of university student go into active life i do not conceal my opinion that book construct on the principle of those which i have cite be more useful than those exclusively found on the more modern system for those student who aim at the mastery of result more difficult and -lrb- in the intellectual sense -rrb- more important the older book be quite insufficient more aspiring student read and generally with much care several part of newton 's principium book i and also book iii -lrb- perhaps the noblest example of geometrical form of cosmical theory that the world have see -rrb- i remember some question from book iii propose in the senate-house examination 1823 in the October term 1819 i go up to the university the work of wood and vince which i have mention still occupy the lecture-room but a great change be in preparation for the university course of mathematics during the great continental war the intercourse between man of science in england and in france have be most insignificant but in the autumn of 1819 three member of the senate -lrb- john herschel george peacock and charle babbage -rrb- have enter into the mathematical society of pari and bring away some of the work on pure mathematics -lrb- especially those of lacroix -rrb- and on mechanic -lrb- principally poisson 's -rrb- in 1820 they make a translation of lacroix 's differential calculus and they prepare a volume of example of the differential and integral calculus these be extensively study : but the form of the college examination or the university examination be not i think influence by they in the winter 1820-1821 or the two follow term but in the winter 1821-1822 peacock be one of the moderator and in the senate-house examination January 1822 he boldly propose a paper of important question entirely in the differential calculus this be consider as establish the new system in the university in January 1823 i think the two system be mingle though i be myself subject to that examination i grieve to say that i have forget much of the detail except that i well remember that some of the question refer to newton book iii on the lunar theory to these i have already allude no other work occur to I as worthy of mention except woodhouse 's lunar theory entirely found on the differential calculus the style of this book be not attractive and it be very little read chapter iii at trinity college cambridge from he take he ba degree to he take charge of the cambridge observatory as plumian professor from January 18th 1823 to march 15th 1828 on jan 30th 1823 i return to cambridge i have already hear that i have gain the 1st smith 's occupy by a set of irregular man who have lose term and be oblige to reside somewhat longer in order to receive the ba degree but at the time of my complete the ba degree -lrb- which be not till some week after the examination and admission -rrb- i with the other complete bachelor be duly invite to the table of the ba scholar and that annoyance end the liberation from undergraduate study leave I with the graduate by take undergraduate pupil mr peacock recommend I to take only four which occupy I four hour every day and for each of they i receive 20 guinea each term my first pupil for the lend and easter term be williamson -lrb- afterwards head master of westminster school -rrb- jame parker -lrb- afterwards qc and vice-chancellor -rrb- bissett and clinton of caius to all these i have be engage before take my ba degree i keep up classical subject i have a set of note on the -lrb- greek : plouto -rrb- and -lrb- greek : nephelaus -rrb- of aristophane finish on mar 15th 1823 and i begin my daily writing of latin as usual on feb 8th in mathematics i work very hard at lunar and planetary theory i have two m book of lunar theory to the 5th order of small quantity which however answer no purpose except that of make I perfectly familiar with that subject i work well upon my quire the figure of saturn suppose homogeneous as affect by the attraction of he ring and the figure of the earth as heterogeneous and the calculus of variation i think it be now that i write a m on constrain motion on mar 17th 1823 i be elect fellow of the cambridge philosophical society on may 9th a cast of my head be take for dr elliotson a active phrenologist by deville a tradesman in the strand i have long think that i should like to visit scotland and on my once say so to my mother she -lrb- who have a most kindly recollection of alnwick -rrb- say in a few word that she think i could not do better i have therefore for some time past fully determine that as soon as i have sufficient spare time and money enough i would go to scotland the interval between the end of easter term and the usual beginning with pupil in the long vacation offer sufficient time and i have now earn a little money and i therefore determine to go and invite my sister to accompany I i have no private introduction except one from jame parker to mr reach a writer of inverness : some which drinkwater send be too late on may 20th we go by coach to stamford thence by pontefract and oulton to york where i see the cathedral which _ then _ disappointed I but i suppose that we be tire with the night journey then by newcastle to alnwick where we stop for the day to see my birthplace on may 24th to edinburgh on this journey i remember well the stone wall between the field the place -lrb- in yorkshire -rrb- where for the first time in my life i see rock the hambleton kyloe cheviot and pentland hill arthur 's seat but still more strikingly the revolving inch keith light at edinburgh i hire a horse and gig for we journey in scotland and we drive by queensferry to kinross -lrb- where for the first time in my life i see cloud on the hill viz on the lomond hill -rrb- and so to perth thence by dunkeld and killicrankie to blair athol -lrb- the dreariness of the drumochter pass make a strong impression on I -rrb- and by aviemore -lrb- where i see snow on the mountain -rrb- to inverness here we receive much kindness and attention from mr reach and after visit the fall of foyer and other sight we go to fort augustus and fort william we ascend ben nevi on which there be a great deal of snow and visit the vitrify fort in glen nevi then by inverary to tarbet and ascend ben lomond from whence we have a magnificent view we then pass by loch achray to glasgow where we find jame parker 's brother -lrb- he father of the house of macinroy and parker be a wealthy merchant of glasgow -rrb- on June 15th to mr parker 's house at blochairn near glasgow -lrb- on this day i hear dr chalmer preach -rrb- and on the 17th go with the family by steamer -lrb- the first that i have see -rrb- to fairly near larg i return the gig to edinburgh visit arran and bute and we then go by coach to carlisle and by penrith to keswick -lrb- by the old road : never shall i forget the beauty of the approach to keswick -rrb- after visit ambleside and kendal we return to cambridge by way of leed and post to bury on the 28th June the expense of this expedition be about _ # 81 _ it open a completely new world to I i have little time to rest at bury in the precede term drinkwater buckle and myself have engage to go somewhere into the country with pupil during the long vacation -lrb- as be customary with cambridge man -rrb- buckle however change he mind drinkwater go to look for a place fix on swansea and engage a house -lrb- call the cambrian hotel keep by a captain jenkin -rrb- on the morning of July 2nd i leave bury for london and by mail coach to bristol on the morning of July 3rd by steamer to swansea and arrive late at night i have then five pupil : parker harman lewi -lrb- afterwards professor in king 's college london -rrb- pierce morton gibson and guest of caius -lrb- afterwards master of the college -rrb- drinkwater have four viz two malkin -lrb- from bury -rrb- elphinstone -lrb- afterwards mp -rrb- and farish -lrb- son of professor farish -rrb- we live a hard-working strange life my pupil begin with I at six in the morning : i be myself read busily we live completely _ en famille _ with two men-servant besides the house establishment one of we first act be to order a four-oared boat to be build fit with a lug-sail : she be call the granta of swansea in the meantime we make sea excursion with boat borrow from ship in the port on July 23rd with a borrow boat we go out when the sea be high but soon find we boat unmanageable and at last get into a place where the sea be break heavily over a shoal and the two of the crew who be nearest to I -lrb- a malkin and lewi -rrb- one on each side be carry out : they be good swimmer and we recover they though with some trouble : the breaker have pass quite over my head : we gain the shore and the boat be take home by land when we own boat be finish we have some most picturesque adventure at the mumble aberavon caswell bay ilfracombe and tenby from all this i learn navigation pretty well the mixture of hard study and open-air exertion seem to affect the health of several of we -lrb- i be one -rrb- : we be cover with painful boil my latin-writing begin again on July 25th : i have note on demosthene lucretius and greek i see that i have work out perfectly the interpolation the effect of uncertainty of longitude & c but i do not see whether i have a final result in jan 1824 at playford i be work on the effect of separate the two lens of a object-glass and on the kind of eye-piece which would be necessary : also on spherical aberration and saturn 's figure on my quire at cambridge i be work on the effect of Saturday -rrb- and to enquire of sir h davy and dr young when i find that succession to the post of astronomer royal be not consider as distinctly a consequence of it i take it coolly and return the next night the whole proposal come to nothing at this time i be engage upon differential equation mountain barometer problem and determination of the height of the gogmagog and several other point
