mutual attraction and by small extraneous force ' and be print in they memoirs i also write a draft of my paper on achromatic eye-piece and on may 17th 1824 it be read at the philosophical society under the title of ` on the principle and construction of the achromatic eye-piece of telescope and on the achromatism of microscope ' include also the effect of separate the lens of the object-glass it be print in they memoirs amongst miscellaneous matter i find that on mar 22nd of this year i begin regularly make extract from the book of the book society a practice which i continue to march 1826 on mar 27th a very rainy day i walk to bury to attend the funeral of my uncle william biddell near diss and on mar 30th i walk back in rain and snow on feb 24th i dine with cubitt in cambridge on may 21st i give a certificate to roger -lrb- the assistant in crosse 's school and my instructor in mathematics -rrb- which my mother amplify much and which i believe procure he election as master of walsall school on June 23rd i go to bury the speech at bury school which i wish to attend take place next day at this point of he autobiography the writer continue now come one of the most important occurrence in my life the important event in question be he acquaintance with richarda smith the lady who afterwards become he wife the courtship be a long one and in the autobiography there be various passage relate to it all write in the most natural and unaffected manner but of somewhat too private a nature for publication it will therefore be convenient to digress from the straight path of the narrative in order to insert a short memoir of the lady who be destine to influence he life and happiness in a most important degree richarda smith be the eldest daughter of the rev richard smith who have be a fellow of trinity college cambridge but be at this time private chaplain to the duke of devonshire and hold the small living of edensor near chatsworth in derbyshire he have a family of two son and seven daughter whom he have bring up and educate very carefully several of he daughter be remarkable both for they beauty and accomplishment richarda smith be now in she 20th year and the writer of the autobiography record that at matlock we receive great attention from mr chenery : in speaking of mr smith i remember he say that mr smith have a daughter whom the duke of devonshire declare to be the most beautiful girl he ever see this be before he have make the acquaintance of the family airy be at this time on a walk tour in derbyshire with he brother william and they be receive at edensor by mr smith to whom he have letter of introduction he seem to have fall in love with miss smith at first sight and within two day of first see she he make she a offer of marriage neither he means nor he prospects at that time permit the least idea of a immediate marriage and mr smith would not hear of any engagement but he never have the least doubt as to the wisdom of the choice that he have make : he work steadily on win fame and position and recommend he suit from time to time to miss smith as opportunity offer and finally marry she nearly six year after he first proposal he constancy have its reward for he gain a most charming and affectionate wife as he record at the time of he marriage my wife be aged between 25 and 26 but she scarcely appear more than 18 or 20 she beauty and accomplishment she skill and fidelity in sketch and above all she exquisite singing of ballad make a great sensation in cambridge they married life last 45 year but the last six year be sadden by the partial paralysis and serious illness of lady airy the entire correspondence between they be most carefully preserved and be a record of a most happy union the letter be write during he numerous journey and excursion on business or pleasure and it be evident that he thought be with she from the moment of they parting every opportunity of write be seize with a energy and avidity that shew how much he heart be in the correspondence nothing be too trivial or too important to communicate to he wife whether relate to family or business matter the letter on both side be always full of affection and sympathy and be write in that spirit of confidence which arise from a deep sense of the value and necessity of mutual support in the trouble of life and with he active and vary employment and he numerous family there be no lack of trouble they be both of they simple-minded sensible and practical people and be very grateful for such comfort and advantage as they be able to command but for nothing in comparison with they deep respect and affection for one another both by natural ability and education she be well qualify to enter into the pursuit of she husband and in many case to assist he she always welcome she husband 's friend and by she skill and attractive courtesy keep they well together she be a admirable letter-writer and in the midst of she numerous domestic distraction always find time for the duty of correspondence in conversation she be very attractive not so much from the wit or brilliancy of she remark as from the brightness and interest with which she enter into the topic under discussion and from the unfailing grace and courtesy with which she attend to the view of other this be especially recognize by the foreign astronomer and man of science who from time to time stay as guest at the observatory and to whom she act as hostess although she be not a accomplished linguist yet she be well able to express herself in french and german and she natural good sense and kindliness place she guest at they ease and make they feel themselves -lrb- as indeed they be -rrb- welcome and at home she father the rev richard smith be a man of most cultivate mind and of the highest principle with a keen enjoyment of good society which the confidence and friendship of he patron the duke of devonshire amply secure to he both at chatsworth and in london he have a deep attachment to he alma mater of cambridge and though not himself a mathematician he have a great respect for the science of mathematics and for eminent mathematician during the long courtship already related mr smith conceive the highest respect for airy 's character as well as for he great repute and attainment and express he lively satisfaction at he daughter 's marriage thus on January 20th 1830 he write to he intended son-in-law as follow : i have little else to say to you than that i continue with heartfelt satisfaction to reflect on the important change about to take place in my dear daughter 's situation a father must not allow himself to dilate on such a subject : of course i feel confident that you will have no reason to repent the irrevocable step you have take but from the manner in which richarda have be bring up you will find such a helpmate in she as a man of sense and affection would wish to have and that she be well prepare to meet the duty and trial -lrb- for such must be meet with -rrb- of domestic life with a firm and cultivate mind and the warm feeling of a kind heart she habit be such as by no means to lead she to expensive wish nor will you i trust ever find it necessary to neglect those study and pursuit upon which you reputation and subsistence be chiefly found to seek for idle amusement for you companion i must indulge no further in speaking of she and have only at present to add that i commit in full confidence into you hand the guardianship of my daughter 's happiness and on April 5th 1830 shortly after they marriage he write to he daughter thus : if thinking of you could supply you place amongst we you would have be with we unceasingly for we have all of we make you the principal object of we thought and we talk since you leave we and i travel with you all you journey to you present delightful home we have all but one feeling of the purest pleasure in the prospect of the true domestic comfort to which we fully believe you to be now go and we rejoice that all you endearing quality will now be employ to promote the happiness of one whom we think so worthy of they as you dear husband who have leave we in the best opinion of he good heart as well as he enlighten and sound understand he late stay with we have endear he to we all never do man enter into the married state from more honorable motive or from a heart more truly seek the genuine happiness of that state than mr airy and he will i trust find he reward in you from all that a good wife can render to the best of husband and he happiness be reflect on yourself it would be difficult to find letter of more genuine feeling and satisfaction or more eloquently express than these the narrative of the autobiography will now be resume i have be disappointed two year before of a expedition to derbyshire i have wish still to make it and my brother wish to go : and we determine to make it this year -lrb- 1824 -rrb- pendulum swing in front of a clock pendulum above and another similarly mount below and that the clock be compare by chronometer compare above carry down and compare compare before leave and bring up and compare the upper and lower pendulum have be interchange it be find now that the reliance on the steadiness of the chronometer be too great and a new method be devise in which for each series the to compare the pendulum directly with the upper one and send they up the shaft : when a inexplicable occurrence stop all proceedings the basket contain all the important instrument be bring up to the surface -lrb- in my presence -rrb- on fire some of the instrument have fall out with they case burn whether a superstitious miner have intentionally fire it or whether the snuff of a candle have be observatory with quetelet -rrb- and pass by cologne frankfort fribourg and basle to zurich thus far we have travel by diligence or post : we now procure a guide and travel generally on foot from the observatory mr lodge be now compel to return to cambridge and i proceed alone by chambry to turin where i make the acquaintance of m plana and see the observatory i then make a tour through north italy look over the observatory at milan padua bologna and florence at leghorn i take a passage for marseille in a xebeque but after sail for three day the weather prove very unfavourable and i land at spezium and proceed by genoa and the cornicus road to marseille at marseille i see m gambart and the observatory and pass by avignon lyon and never to orlans where i visit my old host m legarde thence by pari beauvai and calai to london and cambridge where i arrive on the 30th October i have start with more than _ # 140 _ and return with _ 2 6d _ the expedition be in many way invaluable to I on my return i find various letter from scientific man : some approve of my method for the mass of the moon : some approve highly of my print observation especially d gilbert who inform I that they have produce good effect -lrb- i believe at greenwich -rrb- and herschel -- on nov 13th i give the royal astronomical society a paper about deduce the mass of the moon from observation of venus : on gradually form a course of proceeding which have have a good effect on european astronomy and i be prepare for my marriage on mar 11th i start with my sister to london and arrive at edensor on the and he son on mar 24th i be marry to richarda smith by she father in edensor we stop at edensor till apr 1st and then start in chaise by way of newark and kettering -lrb- where we be in danger of be stop by the snow -rrb- and arrive at cambridge on apr 3rd i be now busy in prepare for lecture especially the part of the optical lecture which related to the theory of interference and polarization i think it be now that my wife draw some of my lecture picture exhibit interference phenomenon my lecture begin on apr 26th and finish on may 24th the number of name be 50 they be consider a excellent course of lecture may 9th be the date of my preface to the 1829 observation : all be then print apparently i do not go to the visitation of the greenwich observatory this year -- i be at this time press tulley the optician about a object-glass for the mural circle -- a new edition of my ` tract ' be want and i prepare to add a tract on the undulatory theory of light in its utmost extent the have then attain whitehall garden _ feb 17 1835 _ sir you probably be aware that in a resolution vote by the house of common in the last session of parliament a opinion be express that pension on the civil list ought not thereafter to be grant by the crown except for the satisfaction of certain public claim among which those rest on scientific or literary eminence be especially mention i trust that no such resolution would have be necessary to induce I as minister of the crown fully to recognize the justice of such claim but i refer to the resolution as remove every impediment to a communication of the nature of that which i be about to make to you in act upon the principle of the resolution in so far as the claim of science be concerned my _ first _ address be make to you and make directly and without previous communication with any other person because it be dictate exclusively by public consideration and because there can be no advantage in or any motive for indirect communication i consider you to have the first claim on the royal favor which eminence in those high pursuit to which you life be devote can give and i fear that the emolument attach to you appointment in the university of cambridge be hardly sufficient to relieve you from anxiety as to the future on account of those in whose welfare you be deeply interested the state of the civil list would enable I to advise the king to grant a pension of three hundred pound per annum and if the offer be acceptable to you the pension shall be grant either to mr airy or yourself as you may prefer i beg you distinctly to understand that you acquiescence in this proposal will impose upon you no obligation personal or political in the slightest degree i make it solely upon public grounds and i ask you by the acceptance of it to permit the king to give some slight encouragement to science by prove to those who may be dispose to follow you bright example that devotion to the highest branch of mathematical and astronomical knowledge shall not necessarily involve they in constant solicitude as to the future condition of those for whom the application of the same talent to more lucrative pursuit would have ensure a ample provision i have the honor to be sir with true respect and esteem you faithful servant robert peel _ mr professor airy & c & c cambridge _ observatory cambridge _ 1835 feb 18 _ sir i have the honor to acknowledge you letter of the 17th acquaint I with you intention of advise the king to grant a pension of _ # 300 _ per annum from the civil list to I or mr airy i trust you will believe that i be sensible of the flattering term in which this offer be make and deeply grateful for the considerate manner in which the principal arrangement be leave to my choice as well as for the freedom from engagement in which you offer leave I i beg to state that i most willingly accept the offer i should prefer that the pension be settle on mr airy -lrb- by which i understand that in case of she survive I the pension would be continue to she during she life or in the contrary event would cease with she life -rrb- i wish that i may have the good fortune to prove to the world that i do not accept this offer without a imply engagement on my part i beg leave again to thank you for you attention and to assure you that the form in which it be convey make it doubly acceptable with sincere respect i have the honor to be sir you very faithful servant gb airy _ the right hon sir robert peel bart first lord of the treasury & c & c _ whitehall _ feb 19th 1835 _ sir i never give a official order which be accompany with more satisfaction to myself than this i have the honor to be sir you faithful servant robert peel _ mr professor airy & c & c cambridge _ \* \* \* intimation of the wish of he majesty 's minister to recommend I to the king for the honor of knighthood i beg to assure you that i be most sensible to the liberality which i have experience from the government in other as well as in pecuniary matter and that i be very highly gratify by the consideration -lrb- undeserved by I i fear -rrb- which they have display in the present instance and if i now request permission to decline the honor offer to I i trust i may make it fully understand that it be not because i value it lightly or because i be not anxious to receive honor from such a source the unalterable custom of this country have attach a certain degree of light consideration to title of honor which be not support by considerable fortune or at least it call for the display of such a establishment as may not be conveniently support by even a comfortable income the provision attach to my official situation and the liberality of the king towards one of the member of my family have place I in a position of great comfort these circumstance however have bind I to consider myself as the devoted servant of the country and to debar myself from effort to increase my fortune which might otherwise have be open to I i do not look forward therefore to any material increase of income and that which i enjoy at present be hardly sufficient in my opinion to support respectably the honor which you and lord john russell have propose to confer upon I for this reason only i beg leave most respectfully to decline the honor of knighthood at the present time i have only to add that my service will always be at the command of the government in any scientific subject in which i can be of the smallest use i be my dear sir you very faithful servant gb airy _ the right honorable t spring rice _ \* \* \* \* \* in brief revision of the year from 1827 to 1835 i may confine myself to the two principal subject -- my professorial lecture and my conduct of the cambridge observatory the lecture as begin in 1827 include ordinary mechanic ordinary hydrostatic and pneumatic -lrb- i think that i do not touch or touch very lightly on the subject connect with the hydraulic ram -rrb- and ordinary optics -lrb- with a very few word on polarization and depolarization -rrb- in 1828 the two first be generally improve and for the third -lrb- optics -rrb- i introduce a few word on circular polarization i believe that it be in 1829 that i make a addition to the syllabus with a small engrave shew the interference of light in the best practical experiment -lrb- that of the flat prism -rrb- and i go thoroughly into the main point of the undulatory theory interference diffraction & c in 1830 i believe i go -lrb- in addition to what be mention above -rrb- into polarization and depolarization of all kind my best lecture diagram be draw and paint by my wife the lecture be universally pronounced to be valuable the subject undergo no material change in 1831 2 3 4 5 and i believe it be a matter of sincere regret to many person that my removal to greenwich terminate the series each lecture nominally occupy a hour but i always encouraged student to stop and talk with I and this supplement be usually consider a valuable part of the lecture practically the lecture on most day occupy two hour i enjoy the lecture much : yet i feel that the labor -lrb- in addition to other work -rrb- make a impression on my strength and i become at length desirous of terminate they the observatory when i take charge of it have only one instrument -- the transit-instrument the principle however which i lay down for my own direction be adapt to the expect complete equipment planet -lrb- totally neglect at greenwich -rrb- be to be observe observation be to be reduce completely and the reduction be to be exhibit in a orderly way : this be a novelty in astronomy i consider it so important that i actually propose to omit in my publication the original observation but be dissuade by herschel and other i sometimes suspend observation for a short time in order to obtain leisure for the reduction i have at first no intention of correct the place of the fundamental star as settle at greenwich but i find myself compel to do so because they be not sufficiently accurate and then i take the course of observe and reduce as a independent observer without reference to any other observatory i introduce the principle of not correct instrumental error but measure they and apply numerical correction i determine my longitude by chronometer and my latitude by a repeat circle borrow from mr sheepshank which i use so well that the result be only half a second in error the form of my reduction in the publish volume for 1828 be rather irregular but the matter be good : it soon attract attention in 1829 the process be much the same : i have a assistant mr baldrey in 1830 still the same with the addition : -- that i formally give the correction of relative right-ascension of fundamental star -lrb- without alteration of equinox which i have not the means of obtain -rrb- to be use in the year 1831 and that i reduce completely the observe occultation -lrb- with a small error subsequently correct -rrb- in 1831 the system of correction of broken transit be improve : the error of assume ra of fundamental star be exhibit : mean solar time be obtain from sidereal time by time of transit of -lrb- symbol : aries -rrb- -lrb- compute by myself -rrb- : the method of compute occultation be improve in 1832 the small equatoreal be erect and be soon employ in observation of the elongation of the 4th satellite of jupiter for determine the mass of jupiter the mural circle be erect at the end of the year but not use the calculation of ra of fundamental star be make homogeneously with the other : separate result of all be include in ledger : a star-catalogue be form : all as to the present time -lrb- 1871 -rrb- with the equatoreal the difference of npd of mar and star be observe with the beginning of 1833 the mural circle be establish at work a second assistant -lrb- mr glaisher -rrb- be appoint and the observatory might be consider complete i make experiment on the graduation of the circle i detect and be annoy by the r -- d i determine the latitude i exhibit the separate result for npd of star in ledger and they means in catalogue i investigate from my observation the place of equinox and the obliquity of the ecliptic i make another series of observation of jupiter 's 4th satellite for the mass of jupiter i observe the solar eclipse with the equatoreal by a method then first introduce which i have since use several time at cambridge and greenwich with excellent effect the moon and the planet be usually observe till near two in the morning correction for defective illumination apply when necessary the volume be very complete the only deficiency be in the observation of moon and planet through the severe morning hour in 1834 the only novelty be -- examination of the graduation of the declination circle of the equatoreal -lrb- excessively bad -rrb- : observation of a spot on jupiter for rotation and of mar and star in 1835 -lrb- include January 1836 -rrb- there be a more complete examination of the equatoreal graduation : parallax and refraction for equatoreal observation : a spot on jupiter : a series of observation on jupiter 's 4th satellite for the mass of jupiter : mar and star : halley 's comet -lrb- the best series of observation which could be make in the season -rrb- : and a short series of meteorological observation on a plan suggest by sir john herschel then at the cape of good hope i can not tell precisely in which year i introduce the follow useful custom towards the end of each year i procure a pocket-book for the following year with a space for every day and carefully examine all the source of element of observation and determine the observation to be make every day i insert they in the pocket-book this system give wonderful steadiness to the plan of observation for the next year the system have be maintain in great perfection at the observatory of greenwich -lrb- the first of these pocket-book which prof adam have find be that for 1833 -rrb- print skeleton form be introduce for all calculation from 1828 in the greenwich observatory library there be a collection i believe complete of print papers commence with my manifesto and contain all syndicate report except for 1833 -lrb- when perhaps there be none -rrb- it seem from these that my first write report on observation & c be on may 30th 1834 the first syndicate report be on may 25th 1829 \* \* \* \* \* a few remark on airy 's private life and friend during he residence at cambridge observatory may be be inter in halley 's tomb in lee churchyard \* \* \* \* \* the follow letter be write by airy in support of the application for a pension to mr pond who have be leave in great distress : to henry warburton esq the point upon which in my opinion mr pond 's claim to the gratitude of astronomer be found be principally the follow _ first _ and chief the accuracy which he introduce into all the principal observation this be a thing which from its nature it be extremely difficult to estimate now so long after the change have be make and i can only say that so far as i can ascertain from book the change be one of very great extent : for certainty and accuracy astronomy be quite a different thing from what it be and this be mainly due to mr pond the most striking exemplification of this be in he laborious work out of every conceivable cause or indication of error in the circle and the two circle : but very great praise be also due for the new system which he introduce in work the transit in compare mr pond 's system of observation with dr maskelyne 's no one can avoid be impress with the inferiority of dr maskelyne 's it be very important to notice that the continental observatory which have since attract so much attention do not at that time exist or do not exist in vigor _ secondly _ the attention bestow by mr pond on those point -lrb- chiefly of sidereal astronomy -rrb- which he regard as fundamental : to which such mass of observation be direct as entirely to remove the doubt from probable error of individual observation or chance circumstance which have injure many other determination _ thirdly _ the regularity of observation the effect of all these have be that since the commencement of mr pond 's residence at greenwich astronomy consider as a accurate representation of the state of the heaven in the most material point have acquire a certainty and a extent which it never have before there be no period in the history of the science so clean on some matter -lrb- in regard to the choice of observation -rrb- i might say that my own judgment would have differ in some degree from mr pond 's but one thing could have be gain only by give up another and upon the general accuracy no improvement could have be make mr pond understand nothing of physical astronomy but neither do anybody else in england the suppose decrease of general efficiency in the last few year be to be ascribe to the follow cause : 1 mr pond 's ill health 2 the inefficiency of he first assistant 3 the oppression of business connect with chronometer the last of these as i have reason to think operate very far business of this nature which -lrb- necessarily -rrb- be _ daily _ and _ peremptory _ will always prevail over that which be _ general _ and _ confidential _ i will not trouble you with a account of the various way in which the chronometer business teaze the astronomer royal -lrb- several alteration have be make at my representation -rrb- but shall merely remark that much of the business have no connection whatever with astronomy i beg to submit these remark to you perusal request you to point out to I _ what part _ of they should be lay before any of the king 's minister _ at what time in what shape _ and to whom address i be quite sure that mr pond 's claim require nothing to ensure favourable consideration but the impression of such a feeling of mr pond 's astronomical merit as must be entertain by any reasonable astronomer and i be most anxious to assist in convey this impression of private history : i go to suffolk for a airy especially place on the border mention in scott 's poem which always have a great attraction for he he also attend a meeting of the british association at glasgow and make a statement regard the planetary and lunar reduction : and look at a site for the glasgow observatory -rrb- in November i go for a short time to cambridge and to keysoe -lrb- my brother 's residence -rrb- on dec 26th my 23 _ on Wednesday -lrb- yesterday -rrb- go with my uncle to the eye estate to see the effect of the fire the farming building of every kind be as completely clear away as if they have be mow down : not a bit of anything but one or two short brick wall and the brick foundation of the barn and stack the aspect of the place be much change because in approach the house you do not see it upon a back-ground of barn & c but standing alone the house be in particularly neat and good order i do not think it at all worth while to make troublesome enquiry of the people who reside there but take mr case 's account there seem no doubt that the fire be cause by the maid-servant throw cinder into a sort of muck-place into which they have be commonly throw i suppose there be after all this dry weather straw or muck drier than usual and the cinder be hotter than usual the whole be on fire in a exceedingly short time and everything be down in less than a hour two engine come from eye and all the population of the town -lrb- as the fire begin shortly after two o'clock in the afternoon -rrb- it be entirely owe to these that my house and the farm -lrb- sewell 's -rrb- on the opposite side of the road be not burn down at the beginning of the fire the wind be ne which blow directly towards the opposite farm -lrb- sewell 's -rrb- : although the nearest part of it -lrb- tiled dwelling house -rrb- be 100 yard off or near it and the great barn -lrb- thatched roof -rrb- considerably further yet both be set on fire several time all this while the tail of my house be grow very hot : and shortly after the building fall in burn ruin the wind change to nw blow directly to my house if this change have happen while the building be stand and burn there would have be no possibility of save the house as it be the solder be melt from the window next the farm-yard and the roof be set on fire in three or four place one engine be keep work on my house and one on the opposite farm a large pond be pretty nearly empty mr case 's horse and bullock be get out not without great difficulty as the progress of the fire be fearfully rapid a sow and nine pig be burnt and a large hog run out burnt so much that the people kill it immediately \* \* \* \* \* george inn winchester _ 1840 June 21 _ at winchester we establish ourselves at the george and then without delay proceed to st cross i do not know before the nature of its hospital establishment but i find that it be a veritable set of alms-house the church be a most curious specimen of the latest norman i never see one so well mark before -- norman ornament on pointed arch pilaster detach with cushion capital and various sign : and it be clearly a instance of that state of the style when people have be force by the difficulty and inelegancy of the round arch in groin to adopt pointed arch for groin but have not learn to use they for windowsthi morning after breakfast go to the cathedral -lrb- look by the way at a curious old cross in the street -rrb- i think that its inside be wholly norman and be most agreeably surprise by find the whole inside groin in every part with excellent late decorate or perpendicular work yet there be several sign about it which lead I to think that the whole inside have be norman and even that the pilaster now work up into the perpendicular be norman the transept be most massive old norman with side-aisle run round they end -lrb- which i never see before -rrb- the groining of the side aisle of the nave very effective from the strength of the cross rib the clerestory window of the quire very large the organ be on one side but the best thing about the quire be the wooden stall-work of early decorate very beautiful a superb lady chapel of early english \* \* \* \* \* portsmouth _ 1840 June 23 _ we leave winchester by evening train to the dolphin southampton and sleep there at nine in the morning we go by steamboat down the river to ryde in the isle of wight : we steamer be go on to portsmouth but we think it better to land at ryde and take a boat for ourselves we then sail out -lrb- rather a blow day -rrb- to the vessel attend col pasley 's operation and after a good deal of go from one boat to another -lrb- the sea be so rough that we boat could not be get up to the ship -rrb- and a good deal of wait we get on board the barge or lump in which col pasley be here we have the satisfaction of see the barrel of gunpowder lower -lrb- there be more than a ton of gunpowder -rrb- and see the diver go down to fix it dress in they diving helmet and supply with air from the great air-pump above when all be ready and the diver have ascend again the barge in which we be be warped away and by a galvanic battery in another barge -lrb- which we have see carry there and whose connection with the barrel we have see -rrb- upon signal give by sound of trumpet the gunpowder be fire the effect be most wonderful the firing follow the signal instantaneously we be at between 100 and 200 yard from the place -lrb- as i judge -rrb- and the effect be as follow as soon as the signal be give there be a report louder than a musket but not so loud as a small cannon and a severe shock be feel at we foot just as if we barge have strike on a rock almost immediately a very slight swell be perceive over the place of the explosion and the water look rather foamy : then in about a second it begin to rise and there be the most enormous outbreak of spray that you can conceive it rise in one column of 60 or 70 foot high and broad at the base resemble a stumpy sheaf with jagged mass of spray spread out at the side and seem to grow outwards till i almost fear that it be come to we it sink i suppose in separate part for it do not make any grand squash down and then there be see log of wood rise and a dense mass of black mud which spread gradually round till it occupy a very large space fish be stun by it : we boatman pick up some it be say by all present that this be the best explosion which have be see : it be truly wonderful then we sail to portsmouththe explosion be a thing worth go many mile to see there be many yacht and sailing boat out to see it -lrb- i count 26 before they be at the fullest -rrb- so that the scene be very gay \* \* \* \* \* here be some note on york cathedral after the fire : red lion hotel redcar _ 1840 sept 7 _ my first letter be close the 1840 observation go to press on may 18th 1842 on aug 18th come into operation a new organization of assistant ' hour of attendance & c require for bring up reduction i work hard myself and my example have good effect he reference to this subject in he report to the visitor be as follow : i have in one of the precede article allude to the backwardness of we reduction in those which follow it i trust that i have sufficiently explain it to say nothing of the loss from ill health of the service of most efficient assistant i be certain that the quantity of current work will amply explain any backwardness perhaps i may particularly mention that in the observation of 1840 there be a unusual quantity of equatoreal observation and the reduction attend these occupy a very great time but as regard myself there have be another cause the reduction of the ancient lunar and planetary observation the attention to chronometer construction the propose management of the printing of papers relate to important operation at the cape of good hope these and similar operation have take up much of my time i trust that i be do well in render greenwich even more distinctly than it have be heretofore the place of reference to all the world for the important observation and result of observation on which the system of the universe be found as regard myself i have be accustom in these matter to lay aside private consideration to consider that i be not a mere superintendent of current observation but a trustee for the honor of greenwich observatory generally and for its utility generally to the world nay to consider myself not as mere director of greenwich observatory but -lrb- however unworthy personally -rrb- as british astronomer require sometimes by my office to interfere -lrb- when no personal offence be give -rrb- in the concern of other establishment of the state if the board support I in this view there can be little doubt that the present delay of computation relate to current observation will be consider by they as a very small sacrifice to the important advantage that may be gain by proper attention to the observation of other time and other place of private history : in the lower of the above description of my present place of abode be the correct one as i fearlessly assert on the authority of diver direction-post on the road lead to it -lrb- by the bye this support endeavor to negociate for a breakfast at six o'clock to-morrow morning it be rain cat and dog \* \* \* \* \* luce 's hotel weymouth _ 1842 feb 27 _ this morning when i get up i find that it be blow determine to go to see the surf on the chesil bank i start with my great-coat on more for defense against the wind than against rain but in a short time it begin to rain and just when i be approach on and mount the bank and descend a little way towards the sea and there be the surf in all its glory i can not give you a idea of its majestic appearance it be evidently very high but that be not the most striking part of it for there be no such thing as go within a considerable distance of it -lrb- the occasional outbreak of the water advance so far -rrb- so that its magnitude could not be well see my impression be that the height of the surf be from 10 to 20 foot but the striking part be the cloud of solid spray which form immediately and which completely conceal all the other operation of the water they rise a good deal higher than the top of the surf so the state of thing be this a great swell be see come grow steeper and steeper then it all turn over and you see a face just like the picture of fall of niagara but in a little more than one second this be totally lose and there be nothing before you but a enormous impenetrable cloud of white spray in about another second there come from the bottom of this cloud the foam current of water up the bank and it return grate the pebble together till they jar penetrate the very brain i stand in the face of the wind and rain watch this a good while and should have stand longer but that i be so miserably wet it appear to I that the surf be higher farther along the bank but the air be so thicken by the rain and the spray that i could not tell when i return the bad weather abate i have now borrow somebody else 's trowser while mine be dry -lrb- have get little wet in other part thanks to my great-coat which successfully bring home a hundredweight of water -rrb- and do not intend to stir out again except perhaps to post this letter \* \* \* \* \* flamsteed house _ 1842 may 15 _ yesterday after post the letter for you i go per that the commissioner ' report have take a very good line of discrimination \* \* \* \* \* 1843 in march i write to dr wynter -lrb- vice-chancellor -rrb- at oxford request permission to see bradley 's with meridional instrument the most important addition to the observatory since its foundation the board of visitor recommend it to the admiralty and the admiralty sanction the construction of the instrument and the building to contain it the follow passage be quote from the address of the astronomer royal to the board of visitor at the special meeting of nov 10th 1843 : the most important object in the institution and maintenance of the royal observatory have always be the observation of the moon in this term i include the determination of the place of fix star which be necessary for ascertain the instrumental error applicable to the instrumental observation of the moon these as regard the object of the institution be merely auxiliary : the history of the circumstance which lead the government of the day to supply the fund for the construction of the observatory shew that but for the demand of accurate lunar determination as aid to navigation the erection of a national observatory would never have be think of and this object have be steadily keep in view when other -lrb- necessary as fundamental auxiliary -rrb- be pass by thus during the latter part of bradley 's time and bliss 's time -lrb- which two period be the least efficient in the modern history of the observatory -rrb- and during the latter part of maskelyne 's presidency -lrb- when for year together there be scarcely a single observation of the declination of a star -rrb- the observation of the moon be keep up with the utmost regularity and the effect of this regularity as regard its peculiar object have be most honorable to the institution the exist theory and table of the moon be found entirely upon the greenwich observation the observatory of greenwich have be look to as that from which alone adequate observation can be expect and from which they will not be expect in vain : and it be not perhaps venture too much to predict that unless some gross dereliction of duty by the manager of the observatory should occur the lunar table will always be found on greenwich observation with this impression it have long be to I a matter of consideration whether means should not be take for render the series of observation of the moon more complete than it can be make by the means at present recognize in we observatory -- in illustration of the foregoing remark the original inscription still remain on the outside of the wall of the octagon room of the observatory may be quote it run thus : ` carolus ii 's rex optimus astronomiae et nauticae artis patronus maximus speculam hanc in be 12m 005 24m 3117 41m 2325 the geodetic longitude compute from element which i publish long ago in the encyclopedia metropolitana be 12m 034 24m 3147 41m 2306 it appear from this that the element to which i have allude represent the form of the earth here as nearly as be possible on the whole i think it probable that this be the best arc of parallel that have ever be measure with regard to the maine boundary : on may 7th col estcourt the british commissioner write to I describe the perfect success of follow out my plan : the line of 64 mile be cut by direction lay out at the two end and the cutting meet within 341 foot the country through which this line be to pass be describe as surpassing in its difficulty the conception of any european it consist of impervious forest steep ravine and dismal swamp a survey for the line be impossible and a tentative process would have break the spirit of the best man i therefore arrange a plan of operation found on a determination of the absolute latitudes and the difference of longitude of the two extremity the difference of longitude be determine by the transfer of chronometer by the very circuitous route from one extremity to the other and it be necessary to divide the whole arc into four part and to add a small part by measure and bearing when this be finish the azimuth of the line for the two end be compute and mark be lay off for start with the line from both end one party after cut more than forty-two mile through the wood be agreeably surprise on the brow of a hill at see directly before they a gap in the wood on the next line of hill it open gradually and prove to be the line of the opposite party on continue the line till they pass abreast of each other they distance be find to be 341 foot to form a estimate of the magnitude of this error it be to be observe that it imply a error of only a quarter of a second of time in the difference of longitude and that it be only one-third -lrb- or nearly so -rrb- of the error which would have be commit if the spheroidal form of the earth have be neglect i must point out the extraordinary merit of the officer who effect this operation transit be observe and chronometer be interchange when the temperature be lower than 19  below zero : and when the native assistant though pay highly desert on account of the severity of the weather the british officer & c -- pearce 's hotel falmouth _ 1845 June 12th Thursday _ then we walk to the unite mine in gwennap the day be very fine and now it be perfectly broiling : and the hill here be long and steep at the unite mine we find the captain and he invite we to join in a rough dinner to which he and the other captain be go to sit down then we examine one of the great pump engine which be consider the best in the country : and some other engine between 3 and 4 there be to be a set out of some work to the man by a sort of dutch auction -lrb- the usual way of set out the work here -rrb- : some refuse ore be to be break up and make marketable and the subject of competition be for how little in the pound on the gross produce the man would work they up while we be here a man be bring up who be hurt in blast : a piece of rock have fall on he at this mine besides the ladder way they have bucket slide in guide by which the man be bring up : and they be just prepare for work another apparatus which they say be try successfully at another mine -lrb- tresavean -rrb- : there be two wooden rod _ a _ and _ b _ reach from the top to the bottom move by crank from the same wheel so that one go up when the other go down and vice vers : each of these rod have small stage at such a distance that when the rod _ a _ be down and the rod _ b _ be up the first stage of _ a _ be level with the first stage of _ b _ : but when the rod _ a _ be up and the rod _ b _ be down the second stage of _ a _ be level with the first stage of _ b _ : so a man who want to descend step on the first stage of _ a _ and wait till it go down : then he step sideways on the first stage of _ b _ and wait till it go down : then he step sideways to the second stage of _ a _ and wait till it go down and so on : or if a man be come up he do just the same while we be here mr r taylor come we walk home -lrb- a long step perhaps seven mile -rrb- in a very hot sun go to tea to mr alfr fox who have a house in a beautiful position look to the outside of falmouth harbor \* \* \* \* \* penzance _ 1845 June 14 Saturday _ yesterday morning we breakfast early at falmouth and before 9 start towards gwennap i have ascertain on Thursday that john william -lrb- the senior of a very wealthy and influential family in this country -rrb- be probably return from london so we drive first to he house burntcoose or barncoose and find he and he wife at home -lrb- they be quaker the rest of the family be not -rrb- sedgwick and whewell and i or some of we party include I have sleep once at they house they receive george and I most cordially and press we to come and dine with they after we visit to tresavean mine of which intention i speak in my last letter : so i name 4 o'clock as hour for dinner after a little stay we drive to tresavean where i find the captain of the mine prepare to send a underground captain and a pit-man to descend with we so we change we clothes and descend by the ladder in the pumpshaft pretty work to descend with the huge pump-rod -lrb- garnish with large iron bolt -rrb- work violently make stroke of 12 foot close to we elbow and with a nearly bottomless pit at the foot of every ladder where we have to turn round the foot of the ladder walk on only a narrow board however we get down to the bottom of the mine with great safety and credit see all the mighty machinery on the way to a greater depth than i ever reach before namely 1900 foot from the bottom of the pump we go aside a short distance into the lowest workings where two man nearly naked be drive a level towards the lode or vein of ore here i feel a most intolerable heat : and upon move to get out of the place i have a dreadful feeling of feebleness and faint such as i never have in my life before the man urge I to climb the ladder to a level where the air be better but they might as well have urge I to lift up the rock i could do nothing but sit down and lean faint against the rock this arise entirely from the badness of the air after a time i feel a trifle better and then i climb one short ladder and sit down very faint again when i recover two man tie a rope round I and go up the ladder before I support a part of my weight and in this way i ascend four or five ladder -lrb- with long rest between -rrb- till we come to a level 260 fathom below the adit or nearly 300 fathom below the surface where there be a tolerable current of pretty good air here i speedily recover though i be a little weak for a short time afterwards george also feel the bad air a good deal but not so much as i he descend to some workings equally low in another place -lrb- towards which the party that i speak of be direct they work -rrb- but say that the air there be by no means so bad we all meet at the bottom of the man-engine 260 fathom below the adit we sit still a little while and i acquire sufficient strength and nerve so that i do not feel the slightest alarm in the operation of ascend by the man-engine this be the funniest operation that i ever see : it be the only absolute novelty that i have see since i be in the country before : it have be introduce 2-1\/2 year in tresavean and one day in the unite mine in my last letter i describe the principle in the actual use there be no other motion to be make by the person who be ascend or descend than that of step sideways each time -lrb- there be proper hand-hold -rrb- with no exertion at all except that of step exactly at the proper instant : and not the shadow of unpleasant feeling in the motion any woman may go with the most perfect comfort if she will but attend to the rule of step and forget that there be a open pit down to the very bottom of the mine in this way we be pump up to the surface and come up as cool as cucumber instead of be drench with perspiration in my description in last letter i forget to mention that between the stage on the move rod which i have there describe there be intermediate stage on the move rod -lrb- for which there be ample room inasmuch as the interval between the stage on each rod use by one person be 24 foot -rrb- and these intermediate stage be use by person _ descend _ : so that there be person _ ascend _ and person _ descend _ at the same time who never interfere with each other and never step on the same stage but merely see each other pass on the other rod -- it be a most valuable invention we then change we clothes and wash and drive to barncoose arrive in good time for the dinner i find myself much restore by some superb sauterne with water when we be propose to go on to camborne mr and mr william press we so affectionately to stop that we at length decide on stop for the night only bargaining for a early breakfast this morning this morning after breakfast we start for redruth and camborne the population between they have increase immensely since i be here before & c & c \* \* \* \* \* here be a letter write to he wife while he be engage on the business of the \* \* \* the follow extract be from a letter write to mr murray for insertion in he handbook of france relate to the breakwater at cherbourg which airy have visit during he journey in france in the autumn of this year royal observatory greenwich _ 1845 oct 8th _ my opinion on the construction i need not say ought not to be quote : but you be quite welcome to find any general statement on it or perhaps it may guide you in further enquiry to make it clear i must speak rather generally upon the subject there be three way in which a breakwater may be construct 1 by build a strong wall with perpendicular face from the bottom of the sea 2 by make a bank with nothing but slope towards the sea 3 by make a sloping bank to a certain height and then build a perpendicular wall upon it -- now if the 1st of these construction could be arrange i have no doubt that it would be the best of all because a sea do not _ break _ against a perpendicular face but recoil in a unbroken swell merely make a slow quiet push at the wall and not make a violent impact but practically it be nearly impossible the 2nd construction make the sea to break tremendously but if the sloping surface be make of square stone put together with reasonable care there be not the smallest tendency to unseat these stone this be the principle of construction of plymouth breakwater in the 3rd construction the slope make the sea to break tremendously and then it strike the perpendicular face with the force of a batter ram : and therefore in my opinion this be the worst construction of all a few face-stone may easily be dislodge and then the sea enter with this enormous force will speedily destroy the whole this be the form of the cherbourg digue from this you will gather that i have a full belief that plymouth breakwater will last very long and that the digue of cherbourg at least its upper wall will not last long the great bank will last a good while gradually suffer degradation but still protect the road pretty well i be assure by the officer reside on the digue that the sea which on breaking be throw vertically upward and then fall down upon the pavement do sometimes push the stone about which be lie there and which weigh three or four ton i see some preparation for the foundation of the fort at the eastern extremity of the digue one artificial stone of concrete measure 12 ' 9  6 ' 7  5 ' 7 and be estimate to weigh 25000 kilogramme chapter vi at greenwich observatory -- 1846 to 1856 1846 on nov 7th i propose a change in the form of estimate for the observatory the original astronomical part be element depend the follow remark extract from the report to the visitor with respect to the instrumental equipment of the observatory embody the view of the astronomer royal at this time : the utmost change which i contemplate as likely to occur in many year in regard to we meridional instrument be the substitution of instrument of the same class carry telescope of larger aperture the only instrument which as i think may possibly be call for by the demand of the astronomer or the astronomical public be a telescope of the largest size for the observation of faint nebula and minute double star whether the addition of such a instrument to we apparatus would be a advantage be in my opinion not free from doubt the line of conduct for the observatory be sufficiently well trace there can be no doubt that we primary object ought to be the accurate determination of place of the fundamental star the sun the planet and above all the moon any addition whatever to we power or we instrumental luxury which should tend to withdraw we energy from these object would be a misfortune to the observatory of private history : in march i visit prof sedgwick at norwich -- on mar 28th the ` sir henry contain a note of the progress of the ironwork for the menai bridge : edgbaston birmingham _ 1847 apr 22 _ yesterday morning we start between 10 and 11 for stourbridge first to see some clay which be celebrate all over the world as the only clay which be fit to make pot for melting glass & c you know that in all these fiery region fire-clay be a thing of very great importance as no furnace will stand if make of any ordinary brick -lrb- and even with the fire-clay the small furnace be examine every week -rrb- but this stourbridge clay be as superior to fire-clay as fire-clay be to common brick-earth then we go to foster ' puddle and roll work near stourbridge these be on a very large scale : of course much that we see be a repetition of what we have see before but there be slit mill machine for roll the puddled bloom instead of hammer they & c and we have the satisfaction of handle the puddle iron ourselves then we go to another work of the foster not far from dudley where part of the work of the tube bridge for the menaus be go on the foster be i believe the largest iron master in the country and the two principal partner the elder mr foster and he nephew accompany we in all we inspection and stepping from one set of work to another the length of tube bridge which they have in hand here be only 120 foot about 1\/4 of the whole length : and at present they be only busy on the bottom part of it : but it be a prodigious thing i shall be anxious about it then we go to other work of the foster ' at king 's wynford where they have blast furnace : and here after see all other usual thing we see the furnace tap in this district the foster work the 10-yard coal in a way different from any body else : they work out the upper half of its thickness and then leave the ground to fall in : after a year or two this ground become so hard as to make a good safe roof and then they work away the other half : thus they avoid much of the danger and difficulty of work the thick bed all at once the ventilation of these mine scarcely ever require fire and then only what they call lamp those little fire-place which be use for give light at night -lrb- in the northumberland and durham pit they constantly have immense roar fire to make a draught -rrb- then we come home through dudley \* \* \* \* \* during he stay in russium there be a great desire manifest private room for the astronomer royal -- on may 12th i make my first proposal of the reflex zenith tube the principle of it be as follow : let the micrometer be place close to the object-glass the frame of the micrometer be firmly connect with the object-glass cell and a reflect eye-piece be use with no material tube pass over the object-glass : and let a basin of quicksilver be place below the object-glass but in no mechanical connection with it at a distance equal to half the focal length of the object-glass such a instrument would at least be free from all uncertainty of twist of plumb-line viscosity of water attachment of upper plumb-line microscope attachment of lower plumb-line microscope and the observation connect with they : and might be expect as a result of this extreme simplicity to give accurate result -- a considerable error be discover in the graduation of troughton 's circle amounting in one part to six seconds which be refer to as follow : ` this instance have strongly confirm I in a opinion which i have long hold -- that no independent division be comparable in general accuracy to engine-division -- where the fundamental division of the engine have be make by troughton 's method and where in any case the determination by the astronomer of error of a few division will suffice in consequence of the uniformity of law of error to give the error of the intermediate division ' -- the method of observe with the altazimuth be carefully describe and the effect of it in increase the number of observation of the moon be thus give for the of the mirror the polishing and the general arrangement be perfectly manage the castle parsonstown _ 1848 aug 30 _ yesterday we be employ entirely about the great telescope beginning rather late the rarely direct to new comet and similar object which sometimes excite considerable interest this omission be intentional it be not because the instrumental means be want -lrb- for we equatoreal though not comparable to those of either cambridge or of pulkowa be fully equal to those usually direct to such object -rrb- but it be because these observation be most abundantly supply from other observatory public and private and because the gain to those observation from we take a part in they would probably be far less than the loss to the important class of observation which we can otherwise follow so well moreover i be unwilling to take any step which could be interpret as attempt to deprive the local and private observatory of honor which they have so nobly earn and finally in this act of abstinence i be desirous of give a example of adhesion to one principle which i be confident might be extensively follow with great advantage to astronomy : -- the principle of division of labor ' -- discovery of small planet be now not infrequent : but the only one of interest to I be melpomene for the follow reason on 1852 June 24 i lose my most dear amiable clever daughter elizabeth the planet have any effect on the weather this enquiry produce the following reply : a subject like that of the occult influence of the planet -lrb- use the word occult in no bad sense but simply as mean not _ thoroughly _ trace -rrb- can be approach in two way -- either by the  priori probability of the existence of such influence or by the  posteriori evidence of they effect if the two can be combine the subject may be consider as claim the dignity of a science even if the effect alone be certain it may be consider that we have a science of inferior degree want however that definiteness of law and that general plausibility which can only be give when true cause in accordance with antecedent experience in other case can be suggest now in regard to the  priori probability of the existence of planetary influence i be far from say that such a thing be impossible the discovery of modern philosophy have all tend to shew that there may be many thing about we unknown even to the scientific world but which well-followed accident reveal with the most positive certainty it be know that every beam of light be accompany by a beam of chemical agency totally undiscoverable to the sens of light or warmth but admit of separation from the luminous and warm ray and produce photogenic effect we know that there be disturbance of magnetism go on about we affect whole continent at a time unknown to man in general but traceable with facility and certainty and which doubtless affect even we brain and nerve -lrb- which be indisputably subject to the influence of magnetism -rrb- now in the face of these thing i will not undertake to say that there be any impossibility or even any want of plausibility in the supposition that body external to the earth may affect we it may well be cite in its favor that it be certain that the sun affect we magnetism -lrb- it be doubtful whether it do so _ im_mediately or mediately by give different degree of warmth to different part of the earth -rrb- and it be believe on inferior evidence that the moon also affect it it may therefore seem not impossible or unplausible that other celestial body may affect perhaps other of the power of nature about we but there i must stop the denial of the impossibility be no assertion of the truth or probability and i absolutely decline to take either side -- either that the influence be real or that the influence be unreal -- till i see evidence of they effect such evidence it be extremely difficult to extract from ordinary fact of observation i have allude to the sun 's daily disturbance of the magnet as one of the most certain of influence yet if you be to observe the magnet for a single day or perhaps for several day you might see no evidence of that influence so completely be it involve with other disturbance whose cause and law be totally unknown i believe that in addition to the effect ascribable to newtonian gravitation -lrb- as general motion of the earth precession of the equinox and tide -rrb- this magnetic disturbance be the only one yet establish as depend on a external body man in general however do not think so it appear to be a law of the human mind to love to trace a effect to a cause and to be ready to assent to any specious cause thus all practical man of the lower class even those whose pecuniary interest be concern in it believe firmly in the influence of the moon upon the wind and the weather i believe that every careful examiner of recorded fact -lrb- among whom i place myself as regard the wind -rrb- have come to the conclusion that the influence of the moon be not discoverable i point out these two thing -lrb- magnetic disturbance and weather -rrb- as tend to shew that notoriety or the assume consent of practical man be of no value the unnotorious matter may be quite certain the notorious matter may have no foundation everything must stand on its own evidence as completely digest and examine of such evidence the planetary influence have not a particle my intended short note have in the course of write grow up into a discourse of very unreasonable length and it be possible that a large portion of it have only increase obscurity at any rate i can add nothing i believe which can help to explain more fully my view on this matter \* \* \* \* \* in this year -lrb- 1862 June 9th -rrb- airy receive the honorary degree of lld in the \* \* \* dear sir i have to thank you for you last letter i do not think everything should be subordinate to the educational element : my idea of a university be that of a place for the cultivation of all science therefore among other science pure mathematics include whatever be interesting as part of this science i be bind therefore to admit that you propose extension of the problem of billiards _ if it _ be find susceptible of interesting mathematical development would be a fit subject of study but in this case i do not think the problem could fairly be object to as puerile -- a more legitimate objection would i conceive be its extreme speciality but this be not a objection that can be bring against modern geometry as a whole : in regard to any particular part of it which may appear open to such a objection the question be whether they be or be not for they own sake or they bearing upon other part of the science to which they belong worthy of be enter upon and pursued but admit -lrb- as i do not -rrb- that pure mathematics be only to be study with a view to natural and physical science the question still arise how be they best to be study in that view i assume and admit that as to a large part of modern geometry and of the theory of number there be no present probability that these will find any physical application but among the remain part of pure mathematics we have the theory of elliptic function and of the jacobian and abelian function and the theory of differential equation include of course partial differential equation now take for instance the problem of three body -- unless this be to be go on with by the mere improvement in detail of the present approximate method -- it be at least conceivable that the future treatment of it will be in the direction of the problem of two fixed centre by means of elliptic function & c and that the discovery will be make not by search for it directly with the mathematical resource now at we command but by prospect for it in the field of these function even improvement in the exist method be more likely to arise from a study of differential equation in general than from a special one of the equation of the particular problem : the material for such improvement which exist in the writings of hamilton jacobus bertrand and bour have certainly so arise and the like remark would apply to the physical problem which depend on partial differential equation i think that the course of mathematical study at the university be likely to be a better one if regulate with a view to the cultivation of science as if for its own sake rather than directly upon consideration of what be educationally best -lrb- i mean that the best educational course will be so obtain -rrb- and that we have thus a justification for a thorough study of pure mathematics in my own limited experience of examination the fault which i find with the man be a want of analytical power and that whatever else may have be in defect pure mathematics have certainly not be in excess i remain dear sir yours sincerely a cayley cambridge _ 10th dec 1867 _ \* \* \* \* \* _ 1867 December 17 _ my dear sir since receive you would mention a supplemental mechanism which i have myself introduce into some chronometer i have long remark that in ordinary good chronometer the freedom from irregularity depend on mechanical cause and the vorstand of the astronomische gesellschaft _ chapter x at the white house greenwich from he resignation of office on August 15th 1881 to he death on January 2nd 1892 history of he life after he resignation of office on the 16th of August 1881 airy leave the observatory which have be he residence for nearly 46 year and remove to the white house whatever he feeling may have be at the sever of he old association he carefully keep they to himself and enter upon he new life with the cheerful composure and steadiness of temper which he possess in a remarkable degree he be now more than 80 year old and the care of office have begin to weigh heavily upon he : the long-continued drag of the transit of venus work have weary he and he be anxious to carry on and if possible complete he numerical lunar theory the great work which for some year have occupy much of he time and attention he mental power be still vigorous and he energy but little impaired : he strong constitution he regular habit of life the systematic relief which he obtain by short holiday expedition whenever he find himself wear with work and he keen interest in history poetry classic antiquity engineering and other subject not immediately connect with he profession have combine to produce this result and in leave office he have no idea of leave off work he resignation of office merely mean for he a change of work it be needless to say that he interest in the welfare and progress of the observatory be as keen as ever he advice be always at the service of he successor and he appointment as visitor a year or two after he resignation give he a official position with regard to the observatory which he much value the white house which be to be he home for the rest of he life be just outside one of the upper gate of the park and about a quarter of a mile from the observatory here he reside with he two unmarried daughter the house suit he well and he be very comfortable there : he prefer to live in the neighborhood with which he be so familiar and in which he be so well know rather than to remove to a distance he daily habit of life be but little altered : he work steadily as formerly take he daily walk on blackheath make frequent visit to playford and occasional expedition to the cumberland lake and elsewhere the work to which he chiefly devoted himself in he retirement be the completion of he numerical lunar theory this be a vast work involve the subtlest consideration of principle very long and elaborate mathematical investigation of a high order and a enormous amount of arithmetical computation the issue of it be unfortunate : he conclude that there be a error in some of the early work which vitiate the result obtain : and although the whole process be publish and be leave in such a state that it would be a comparatively simple task for a future astronomer to correct and complete it yet it be not permit to the original author of it to do this to avoid the necessity of frequent reference to this work in the history of airy 's remain year it will be convenient to summarize it here it be commence in 1872 : on feb 23rd in this year i first -lrb- privately -rrb- form the notion of prepare a numerical lunar theory by substitute delaunay 's number in the proper equation and see what would come of it from this time forward till he power to continue it absolutely fail he pursue the subject with he usual tenacity of purpose during he tenure of office every available opportunity be seize for make progress with he lunar theory and in every report to the visitor a careful statement be insert of the state in which it then stand and after he resignation of office it form the bulk of he occupation in 1873 the theory be form and by 1874 it be so far advance that he publish in the monthly notice of the royal astronomical society a statement of the fundamental point of the theory in 1875 the theory have advance to a stage where extensive arithmetical computation be require he obtain a small grant from the government in aid of the expense of the work and other grant be make in subsequent year by 1878 the calculation be so far advance that a opinion could be form as to the probable accuracy of the theory and the follow remark be make : a cursory collation of the term relate to the area -lrb- in the ecliptic -rrb- lead I to suppose that there might be some error in the computation of the annual equation and related term but no error could be discover and the work proceed the complex character of the theory and the extreme care require in the mathematical process be well illustrate by the follow statement which occur in the report of 1879 a important error in the theoretical formula for variation of radius vector longitude and latitude be discover some calculation depend on they be cancel in 1880 and 1881 the work be continue but be sadly interrupt by the pressure of the transit of venus work and other business after he resignation of the office of astronomer royal he have no further public assistance and do much of the computation himself but a sum of _ # 100 _ be contribute by mr de la rue in furtherance of the work and this sum be spend on computer in he retirement the work make good progress and on dec 31st 1882 he make the following note : i finish and put in general order the final table of equation of variation this be a definite point in the lunar theory i hope shortly to take up severely the numerical operation of the lunar theory from the very begin the work be continue steadily through 1883 and on mar 24th 1884 he make application through the board of visitor to the admiralty to print the work : after the usual enquiry as to the expense this be accede to and copy be send to the printer as soon as it be ready the first print proof be receive on feb 5th 1885 and the whole book be print by the end of 1886 from the frequent reference in he journal to error discover and correct during the progress of these calculation it would seem likely that he power be not what they have be and that there be a probability that some important error might escape correction he be far too honest to blind himself to this possibility and in the preface to he numerical lunar theory he say thus : i have explain above that the principle of operation be to arrange the fundamental mechanical equation in a form suit for the investigation of lunar theory to substitute in the term of these equation the numerical value furnish by delaunay 's great work and to examine whether the equation be thereby satisfy with painful alarm i find that they be not satisfied and that the discordance or failure of satisfy the equation be large the critical trial depend on the great mass of computation in section ii these have be make in duplicate with all the care for accuracy that anxiety could supply still i can not but fear that the error which be the source of discordance must be on my part i can not conjecture whether i may be able to examine sufficiently into this matter he resolutely take in hand the revision of he work and continue it till October 1888 but it be clear from the entry in he journal that he power be now unequal to the task and although from time to time he suspect that he have discover error yet it do not appear that he determine anything with certainty he never doubt as follow : _ 1881 oct 31 _ sir i have to acknowledge you letter of October 17 introduce to my notice the difficulty which appear to be arise in america regard a standard time for extensive use throughout n america applicable to railway traffic only the subject as include consideration of convenience in all the matter to which it apply be one of difficulty probably insuperable the certainty however that objection may be raise to every scheme render I less timid in offer my own remark which be much at you service i first comment upon you expression of standard time applicable to railway traffic only but do you mean this as affect the transaction between one railway and another railway or as affect each railway and the local interest -lrb- temporal and other -rrb- of the town which it touch the difference be so great that i should be dispose to adopt it as mark very strongly the difference to be make between the practice of railway among themselves and the practice of railway towards the public and will base a system on that difference as regard the practice of railway among themselves : if the various railway of america be join and inosculate as they be in england it appear to I indispensable that they have one common standard _ among themselves _ : say washington observatory time but this be only need for the office-transaction between the railway it may be keep perfectly private never communicate to the public at all and i should recommend this as the first step there will then be no difficulty in deduce from these private washington time the accurate local time at those station -lrb- whose longitude be suppose to be fairly well know as a sailor with a sextant can determine one in a few hour -rrb- which the railway authority may deem worthy of that honor generally the terminus of railway thus we shall have a series of basis of local time of authoritative character through the country of such basis _ we _ have two greenwich and dublin : and they be separate by a sea-voyage in the we of america there must be a greater number and probably not so well separate still it be indispensable to adopt such a system of local center no people in this world can be induce to use a reckoning which do not depend clearly upon the sun in all civilized country it depend -lrb- approximately -rrb- on the sun 's meridian passage even the sailor on mid-ocean refer to that phenomenon and the solar passage with reasonable allowance 20m or 30m one way or another must be recognize in all time-arrangement as give the fundamental time the only practical way of do this be to adopt for a whole region the fundamental time of a center of that region and to this fundamental time the local time of the railway as now enter into all the concern of life must be adapt a solicitor have a appointment to meet a client by railway a physician to a consultation how be this to be keep if the railway use one time and every other act of life another there be one chain of circumstance which be almost peculiar -- that of the line from new york to san francisco here i would have two clock at every station : those on the north side all shew san francisco time and those on the south all shew new york time every traveller 's watch would then be available to the end of he journey a system fundamentally such as i have sketch would give little trouble and may i think be adopt with advantage i be sir you faithful servant gb airy _ mr edward barrington _ 1882 he return from playford on jan 17 : he other movement during the year be as follow : from apr 27th to may 11th engineer the british association the royal institution & c he present papers or make communication on subject specially suit to each and in like manner to various newspaper : there be 58 papers in this category in so long a official life there would naturally be a great number of official report parliamentary return & c and these with other miscellaneous papers print for particular object and for a limited circulation amount in all to 141 under this head come he annual report to the board of visitor which in themselves contain a extremely full and accurate history of the observatory during he tenure of office there be 46 of these report and they would of themselves form a large volume of about 740 page the following summary of he print papers shew the manner in which they be distribute
